  • [9/9] mm: zswap: per-node kmem accounting for zswap/zsmalloc Neutral Last comment 2026-06-26 Usama Arif Nhat Pham
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch converts the task_struct's objcg field to a per-node array of obj_cgroup pointers, allowing for efficient and accurate accounting of memory usage across multiple NUMA nodes. The change addresses issues with current_obj_cgroup() returning an objcg whose nid did not match the current CPU's node, defeating per-node vmstat batching and accounting hierarchy. The patch also introduces a new tagged pointer to store the array, ensuring atomic updates and preserving existing race-with-kmem_attach semantics.

Last comment 2026-06-25 [RFC PATCH] mm: bypass swap readahead for zswap Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch bypasses swap readahead for zswap, an in-memory compression backend similar to zram. The current implementation of swap readahead is optimized for traditional disk-based swap devices and can cause issues like inflated anon residency, page cache thrashing, and increased fault latency when applied to an in-memory backend. By skipping readahead for zswap, the patch aims to improve performance by reducing unnecessary prefetching of pages that are not needed. The results from testing this bypass show significant improvements in build time, swap-in/out rates, and reduced swap readahead hits.

Barry Song ↳ replying to Alexandre Ghiti Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Barry Song noted that readahead swap entries in zswap can lead to unnecessary decompression and additional page faults, and suggested a proof-of-concept patch to enable fault-around-like behavior for do_swap_page() when the readahead folios are in zswap.
Show original comment 
Basically, I have been seeing the same issue recently. If the
readahead swap entries are also in zswap, we end up doing the
decompression during one page fault, but then need another page fault
to fetch the page from the swap cache and install the mapping. In that
case, readahead may not be beneficial.

On the other hand, if the readahead swap entries are not in zswap, the
situation is different.

For example, suppose we fault on the swap entry for address 1 MB and
readahead brings in the entry for 1 MB + 4 KB. If both entries are in
zswap, readahead does not seem like a good trade-off. However, if the
1 MB + 4 KB entry is not in zswap and would otherwise require storage
I/O, then readahead can be beneficial.

So I implemented a rather ugly fault_around-like mechanism in
do_swap_page(). At least with page-cluster == 1, I am seeing a
performance improvement, as the readahead folios can be mapped
directly and do not require a second page fault.

It is admittedly quite ugly and is only meant as a proof of concept :-)

Subject: [PATCH PoC] mm: enable do_swap_page fault_around

Signed-off-by: Barry Song (Xiaomi) <baohua@kernel.org>
---
 mm/memory.c | 95 +++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++++

diff --git a/mm/memory.c b/mm/memory.c
index c00a31a6d1d0..1db79f45a575 100644
--- a/mm/memory.c
+++ b/mm/memory.c
@@ -4736,6 +4736,100 @@ static void check_swap_exclusive(struct folio
*folio, swp_entry_t entry,
         } while (--nr_pages);
 }

+static void do_swap_map_around(struct vm_fault *vmf, struct
swap_info_struct *si)
+{
+        struct vm_area_struct *vma = vmf->vma;
+        int nr_around = 1 << page_cluster;
+        unsigned long start = max3(vma->vm_start, vmf->address -
(nr_around - 1) * PAGE_SIZE,
+                        vmf->address & PMD_MASK);
+        unsigned long end = min3(vma->vm_end, vmf->address +
nr_around * PAGE_SIZE,
+                        (vmf->address & PMD_MASK) + PMD_SIZE);
+        unsigned long nr_pages = (end - start) >> PAGE_SHIFT;
+        unsigned long delta_pages = (vmf->address - start) >> PAGE_SHIFT;
+        pte_t *ptep = vmf->pte - delta_pages;
+
+        for (int i = 0; i < nr_pages; i++, ptep++) {
+                unsigned long address = start + (i << PAGE_SHIFT);
+                rmap_t rmap_flags = RMAP_NONE;
+                pte_t orig_pte, pte;
+                struct folio *folio;
+                struct page *page;
+                softleaf_t entry;
+                bool exclusive;
+
+                if (ptep == vmf->pte)
+                        continue;
+                orig_pte = ptep_get(ptep);
+                exclusive = pte_swp_exclusive(orig_pte);
+                if (!exclusive)
+                        continue;
+                entry = softleaf_from_pte(orig_pte);
+                if (!softleaf_is_swap(entry))
+                        continue;
+                folio = swap_cache_get_folio(entry);
+                if (!folio)
+                        continue;
+                if (unlikely(!folio_matches_swap_entry(folio, entry)))
+                        goto skip;
+                if (folio_test_locked(folio))
+                        goto skip;
+                if (!folio_test_uptodate(folio))
+                        goto skip;
+                if (!folio_trylock(folio))
+                        goto skip;
+                if (folio_test_ksm(folio) || folio_test_large(folio) ||
+                        !folio_test_uptodate(folio))
+                        goto unlock;
+                if (exclusive && folio_test_writeback(folio) &&
+                                data_race(si->flags & SWP_STABLE_WRITES))
+                        exclusive = false;
+
+                arch_swap_restore(folio_swap(entry, folio), folio);
+
+                page = folio_page(folio, 0);
+                add_mm_counter(vma->vm_mm, MM_ANONPAGES, 1);
+                add_mm_counter(vma->vm_mm, MM_SWAPENTS, -1);
+                pte = mk_pte(page, vma->vm_page_prot);
+                if (pte_swp_soft_dirty(orig_pte))
+                        pte = pte_mksoft_dirty(pte);
+                if (pte_swp_uffd_wp(orig_pte))
+                        pte = pte_mkuffd_wp(pte);
+
+                if (exclusive) {
+                        if ((vma->vm_flags & VM_WRITE) &&
!userfaultfd_pte_wp(vma, pte) &&
+                                        !pte_needs_soft_dirty_wp(vma, pte)) {
+                                pte = pte_mkwrite(pte, vma);
+                        }
+                        rmap_flags |= RMAP_EXCLUSIVE;
+                }
+                flush_icache_pages(vma, page, 1);
+
+                if (!folio_test_anon(folio)) {
+                        folio_add_new_anon_rmap(folio, vma, address,
rmap_flags);
+                        folio_put_swap(folio, NULL);
+                } else {
+                        folio_add_anon_rmap_ptes(folio, page, 1, vma, address,
+                                        rmap_flags);
+                        folio_put_swap(folio, page);
+                }
+
+                set_ptes(vma->vm_mm, address, ptep, pte, 1);
+                arch_do_swap_page_nr(vma->vm_mm, vma, address,
+                                pte, pte, 1);
+
+                if (should_try_to_free_swap(si, folio, vma, 1, vmf->flags))
+                        folio_free_swap(folio);
+                folio_unlock(folio);
+                swap_update_readahead(folio, vma, address);
+                update_mmu_cache_range(vmf, vma, address, ptep, 1);
+                continue;
+unlock:
+                folio_unlock(folio);
+skip:
+                folio_put(folio);
+        };
+}
+
 /*
  * We enter with either the VMA lock or the mmap_lock held (see
  * FAULT_FLAG_VMA_LOCK), and pte mapped but not yet locked.
@@ -5121,6 +5215,7 @@ vm_fault_t do_swap_page(struct vm_fault *vmf)

         /* No need to invalidate - it was non-present before */
         update_mmu_cache_range(vmf, vma, address, ptep, nr_pages);
+        do_swap_map_around(vmf, si);
 unlock:
         if (vmf->pte)
                 pte_unmap_unlock(vmf->pte, vmf->ptl);

Last comment 2026-06-26 [PATCH v2 0/9] per-memcg-per-node kmem accounting 9 patches
Show 4 individual patches
  • [1/9] memcg: convert task->objcg to a per-node objcgs array
  • [2/9] memcg: charge kmem pages and slab objects against per-node objcg
  • [3/9] mm: percpu: fix obj_exts metadata charge size
  • [4/9] mm: percpu: Split memcg charging and kmem accounting
Neutral LLM

This patch series aims to improve kmem accounting by introducing per-memcg-per-node accounting, which reduces the overhead of discovering each backing page's real node in a many-CPU system. The patches batch memcg accounting and provide alternative versions for different scenarios, including one that caches each backing page's node in the chunk.

On 6/26/26 12:09, Alexandre Ghiti wrote:
> This is version 2 of per-memcg-per-node kmem accounting.
>
> As asked by Joshua, I ran some microbenchmarks to check the impact of
> this fine grain accounting.
>
> TL;DR: There is a substantial impact (up to +337% on small percpu allocations)
> on a benchmark that loops over small percpu allocations. On the other hand,
> on a userspace program that creates a bpf percpu map, this cost is not visible.
>
> I followed Joshua's advice and now this version batches the memcg accounting:
> it improves the performance +337% vs +417% (v1) on 176 cores single node
> machine and +153% vs 206% (v1) on 80 cores 2 nodes machine.
>
> We can see that the overhead of this version scales linearly with the number of
> cpus (the number of nodes being small). This overhead comes mainly from
> vmalloc_to_page() so I have another variant (b) that decreases the impact even
> more (+131% vs +337% on 176 cores and +86% vs +153% on 80 cores) but I'm not
> sure the added complexity is needed so I did not send this version, let me know
> what you think.
>
> Performance
> ===========
>
> All benchmarks run in a memcg with __GFP_ACCOUNT.
>
> 1) BPF percpu map create/destroy, full series vs baseline kernel (two
>     boots, 176-CPU AMD EPYC, 1 NUMA node): the per-node accounting is lost
>     in the BPF syscall overhead, the delta is within noise (us/op):
>
>       size (B):    64     256    1024   4096   8192
>       delta:     -5.5%  -5.1%  -1.8%  -5.1%  -4.1%
>
> 2) In-kernel microbench that isolates the accounting cost: a tight
>     __alloc_percpu_gfp()/free_percpu() loop, __GFP_ACCOUNT on vs off on the
>     same boot (ACCT COST = on - off). The dominant cost on a many-CPU box
>     is discovering each backing page's real node (vmalloc_to_page() per
>     possible CPU). ACCT COST by value size:
>
>     176-CPU EPYC, 1 node
>       size (B):              64       256      1024     4096     8192
>        baseline (upstream)  +5.3%    +5.4%    +0.1%    -1.8%    -0.5%
>        v1 credit (per-page) +417.3%  +182.5%  +68.5%   +21.4%   +16.1%
>     a) per-node accounting  +337.8%  +141.8%  +36.1%   +11.9%   +6.8%
>     b) per-page nid cache   +131.3%  +53.7%   +10.5%   +0.9%    +2.0%
>     c) single-node fast     +12.6%   +12.1%   +3.5%    +6.6%    +0.7%
>
>     80-CPU Xeon Gold 6138, 2 nodes (fast path inactive)
>       size (B):              64       256      1024     4096     8192
>        baseline (upstream)  +1.2%    -3.8%    +12.4%   +1.2%    +0.5%   (noise)
>        v1 credit (per-page) +206.1%  +134.0%  +44.5%   +11.6%   +11.5%
>     a) per-node accounting  +153.2%  +64.7%   +19.4%   +4.2%    +5.9%
>     b) per-page nid cache   +86.5%   +45.5%   +14.7%   +1.8%    +1.6%
>
>     (a) this patchset without fast path for single node
>     (b) is an alternative version, not in this series, that caches each backing
>         page's node in the chunk so the walk is paid once per page instead of
>         once per allocation
>     (c) this patchset with fast path for single node
>
> Changes in v2
> =============
>
> - objcg lifetime: Shakeel's patch 1 now guarantees the lifetime of every
>    per-node objcg
> - dropped patch 5 and 6 since Shakeel's patch 2 replaces them
> - fixed the number of precharged pages (the v1 formula under-precharged)
> - per-node batching (Joshua's suggestion): accumulate the per-node bytes
>    first, then issue one account_kmem()/uncharge() per touched node =>
>    O(nodes) memcg ops instead of O(num_possible_cpus)
> - single-node fast path: skip the per-cpu node walk on single node machines
> - obj_exts metadata is now accounted per-node (walk its vmalloc pages)
>    rather than charged whole to one memcg (Shakeel's main v1 objection).
> - renamed obj_cgroup_get_nid() -> obj_cgroup_nid() (returns a borrowed RCU
>    pointer, no ref taken).
> - zswap: fixed the missing locking around the per-node objcg lookup (now
>    done under RCU + obj_cgroup_tryget()).
>
> This series pursues the work initiated by Joshua [1]. We need kernel
> memory to be accounted on a per-node basis in order to be able to know
> the memcg <-> physical memory association.
>
> This series takes advantage of the recently introduced per-node
> obj_cgroup and makes those obj_cgroup tied to their NUMA node.
>
> The bulk of the series is percpu per-node accounting: percpu
> "precharges" the memcg before we know the actual location of the pages
> it uses, so charging and accounting had to be split. All other kmem
> users (slab, __memcg_kmem_charge_page) are now handled directly by
> Shakeel's per-node obj_cgroup infrastructure this series sits on, so
> only percpu and zswap need explicit per-node work here (zswap support
> is limited because Joshua is working on it in parallel [3]).
>
> Thanks Joshua and Shakeel for the early feedback!
>
> [1] https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/20260404033844.1892595-1-joshua.hahnjy@gmail.com/
> [2] https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/56c04b1c5d54f75ccdc12896df6c1ca35403ecc3.1772711148.git.zhengqi.arch@bytedance.com/
> [3] https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/20260311195153.4013476-1-joshua.hahnjy@gmail.com/
>
> Functional Testing
> ==================
>
> - Tested with a percpu kmem self-test in an 8-node VM (2 nodes with CPUs,
>    6 memory-only). For each allocation it checks that every node is charged
>    and later uncharged the same number of bytes -- including a CPU-less node
>    that ends up holding the obj_exts metadata -- and that nothing is left
>    charged after teardown. All checks pass. (The self-test module is not
>    part of this series.)
>

Last comment 2026-06-22 Re: [v2 15/16] mm: install PMD swap entries on swap-out Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch fixes a problem in the zswap code where PMD swap entries are created before zswap is enabled, leading to errors when trying to read from disk. The fix involves checking if a large folio's range is entirely absent from zswap and returning an error if any slot is still present. This ensures that partially-initialised data is not returned in case of an unexpected failure.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Usama Arif ↳ replying to Alexandre Ghiti Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Usama Arif noted that the current strategy for handling PMD swap entries on zswap enablement can lead to a SIGBUS error, and suggested modifying the code in mm/zswap.c to scan the covered slots before attempting to read from disk. He also mentioned that his local patch is similar to Lance's suggestion in an earlier email.

diff --git a/mm/zswap.c b/mm/zswap.c
index 761cd699e0a3..f94133d876a1 100644
--- a/mm/zswap.c
+++ b/mm/zswap.c
@@ -1592,17 +1592,40 @@ int zswap_load(struct folio *folio)
        if (zswap_never_enabled())
                return -ENOENT;
 
+       entry = xa_load(tree, offset);
+
        /*
         * Large folios should not be swapped in while zswap is being used, as
         * they are not properly handled. Zswap does not properly load large
         * folios, and a large folio may only be partially in zswap.
+        *
+        * If no zswap entry exists for the folio, however, the caller can read
+        * it from the backing swap device. Scan the covered slots so a PMD
+        * swapin racing with zswap enable does not SIGBUS solely because zswap
+        * became available after the PMD swap entry was installed.
         */
-       if (WARN_ON_ONCE(folio_test_large(folio))) {
+       if (folio_test_large(folio)) {
+               unsigned int type = swp_type(swp);
+               bool found = !!entry;
+               long index;
+
+               if (!found) {
+                       for (index = 1; index < folio_nr_pages(folio); index++) {
+                               swp_entry_t cur = swp_entry(type, offset + index);
+
+                               if (xa_load(swap_zswap_tree(cur), offset + index)) {
+                                       found = true;
+                                       break;
+                               }
+                       }
+               }
+               if (!found)
+                       return -ENOENT;
+

Boris Burkov

10 items
Patches Submitted (6)
[PATCH 0/5] allocate extent_buffer GFP_NOFAIL with unlocked retry Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series aims to reduce contention and stalls in the Linux kernel by allowing certain memory allocations, specifically for extent_buffers used by the Btrfs file system, to be retried outside of critical sections when they fail due to lack of memory. The approach taken is to allocate memory with GFP_NOWAIT (which does not block) initially, and if that fails, retry the allocation with GFP_NOFS | GFP_NOFAIL (which will block but guarantees success) without holding any locks. This should help reduce the number of hung tasks and improve system responsiveness.

[PATCH 1/5] btrfs: factor init_extent_buffer from __alloc_extent_buffer Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch refactors the initialization of extent buffers in the Btrfs file system. It separates the initialization code from the allocation code, allowing for more flexibility and easier maintenance. The goal is to prepare for preallocating extent buffer data by allocating it together with other necessary resources. This change will make it easier to implement preallocation in the future.

[PATCH 2/5] btrfs: add struct btrfs_eb_prealloc Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new structure called `struct btrfs_eb_prealloc` to carry the extent_buffer and btrfs_folio_state needed for NOFAIL allocations with retries outside the critical section. The existing allocation pathways are refactored to use this new struct, but no functional changes are made. Empty prealloc structs are wired in from callers. This is part of a larger series of patches to support NOFAIL allocations.

[PATCH 3/5] btrfs: enable unlocked NOFAIL retry for eb allocations Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch enables unlocked NOFAIL retry for eb allocations in the Btrfs file system. It introduces a new preallocation struct to carry allocation and signal requirements, allowing callers to attempt NOWAIT allocations if supported by the allocator. If the initial allocation fails, it signals that preallocation is required, enabling the caller to perform the necessary allocation and retry with the preallocated resources. The patch also notes that two GFP_NOFS allocations remain under lock and cannot be preallocated due to limitations in the filemap xarray.

[PATCH 4/5] btrfs: probe with GFP_NOWAIT for tree block readahead Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch modifies the btrfs readahead mechanism to use GFP_NOWAIT when reading tree blocks, allowing it to fail instead of blocking in direct reclaim if allocation fails. This change is intended to prevent painful stalls and lock contention caused by readahead getting stuck in direct reclaim.

[PATCH 5/5] btrfs: use GFP_NOWAIT when inhibiting eb writeback Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch modifies the btrfs_inhibit_eb_writeback function to use GFP_NOWAIT instead of GFP_NOFS when allocating memory for inhibiting writeback on an extent buffer. This change is made because the allocation is already best effort and occurs while holding locks, making NOWAIT a suitable choice.

Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds atomic whole-device hotplug control to the dax kmem driver via a sysfs 'state' attribute, allowing for transitions across entire regions of memory to be performed atomically and safely. The current implementation has a race condition between offlining and removing memory blocks, which this patch aims to solve by introducing a new ABI with state names mirroring per-block memoryX/state attributes. The series also includes mm preparation changes to support the new hotplug control, including adding helpers for online type management and atomic offline+remove of multiple ranges. The dax/kmem feature is updated to plumb online_type through its device creation path and extract hotplug/hotremove into helper functions. A selftest is added to regression test the new attribute.

[PATCH 1/2] kernel/entry: add CONFIG_SYSCALL_USER_DISPATCH to compile SUD out 2 patches
Show 1 individual patches
  • [2/2] kernel/entry: add kernel.syscall_user_dispatch sysctl
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new configuration option, CONFIG_SYSCALL_USER_DISPATCH, to make Syscall User Dispatch (SUD) an optional feature in the Linux kernel. SUD allows system calls to be redirected to userspace handlers and is required by native foreign-binary emulators like Wine/Proton. The patch introduces this new configuration option to allow minimal or high-security systems to disable SUD if needed, while still making it available for systems that require it.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-10 Last comment 2026-06-23 ⏱ No recent activity [RFC PATCH 0/3] mm/numa: reserve standby NUMA nodes for runtime claiming 3 patches
Show 3 individual patches
  • [1/3] mm/numa: add exclusive node pool and numa=standby boot parameter Neutral Submitted 2026-06-10Last comment 2026-06-23 Mike Rapoport Gregory Price (author)
  • [2/3] acpi/numa: add CONFIG_ACPI_NUMA_STANDBY_NODES Submitted 2026-06-10
  • [3/3] acpi/numa: add CONFIG_ACPI_NUMA_ADD_CFMWS_NODES Submitted 2026-06-10
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series allows Linux to reserve empty 'standby' NUMA nodes at boot, which can be claimed by drivers at runtime and used for memory tiering or isolation. This is useful for scenarios where hotplugged memory needs to be spread across more nodes than the firmware describes. The patch introduces three ways to reserve standby nodes: a boot parameter, two kernel configuration options, and a mechanism to create these nodes after the topology is finalized. The series includes testing under various topologies and configurations.

Last comment 2026-06-27 [PATCH v2] mm/vmstat: fold stranded per-cpu node stats when a node comes online Neutral LLM

This patch addresses a long-standing issue where per-cpu node statistics are not properly folded when a node comes online, leading to permanent skew in the vmstat counters. The existing code zeroes out the per-cpu counters, causing a permanent loss of data. This patch folds the stranded deltas before the node rejoins the online set, ensuring accurate and consistent statistics.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Andrew Morton ↳ replying to Gregory Price Needs Work LLM
Raised concerns about the safety of folding stranded per-cpu node statistics during offlining, suggesting that it could lead to deadlocks or other issues. Also questioned the need for visiting every possible CPU.
Show original comment 
On Sat, 27 Jun 2026 16:22:43 -0400 Gregory Price <gourry@gourry.net> wrote:

> A per-node vmstat counter is pgdat->vm_stat[] plus per-cpu deltas.
> A balanced counter can sit split as global=+N / per-cpu=-N.
> 
> The folds reconciling the split only walk online nodes, so when
> try_offline_node() marks a node offline the per-cpu deltas are stranded.
> 
> A subsequent online resets the per-cpu area but not pgdat->vm_stat[],
> orphaning the +N permanently.  All NR_VM_NODE_STAT_ITEMS are affected.

Geeze, simple mistake, been there ten years...

> The existing code zeroes the per-cpu counters and causes a permanent
> skew. Fold the stranded deltas instead, before the node rejoins the
> online set. The node is not online yet and the hotplug lock is held,
> so the remote access to per-cpu values is safe.

> -		p = per_cpu_ptr(pgdat->per_cpu_nodestats, cpu);
> +		for (i = 0; i < NR_VM_NODE_STAT_ITEMS; i++)

and that's a lot of items.

I guess the overall loop count won't be large enough to cause issues,
but it's large!

Perhaps there's some simple test we can do on the per_cpu_nodestat to
avoid the inner loop?  Perhaps might need to add a field for this?

btw, "for(int i..." is allowed nowadays.  It'll make this code nicer, IMO.

And... Sashiko seems to have found a pre-existing issue:
	https://sashiko.dev/#/patchset/20260627202243.758289-1-gourry@gourry.net

> +			if (p->vm_node_stat_diff[i])
> +				node_page_state_add(p->vm_node_stat_diff[i], pgdat, i);
>  		memset(p, 0, sizeof(*p));
>  	}
Signals: NEEDS_WORK
Gregory Price Neutral LLM
Responded to Andrew Morton's concerns, explaining the difficulties of safely folding stranded per-cpu node statistics during offlining and the potential risks of visiting every possible CPU.
Show original comment 
On Sat, Jun 27, 2026 at 04:10:07PM -0700, Andrew Morton wrote:
> 
> > The existing code zeroes the per-cpu counters and causes a permanent
> > skew. Fold the stranded deltas instead, before the node rejoins the
> > online set. The node is not online yet and the hotplug lock is held,
> > so the remote access to per-cpu values is safe.
> 
> Oh.  Shouldn't we be doing this during offlining?
>

I tried this first.  I was unable to convince myself there was a 
safe way to accomplish this.

1) sashiko pointed out we can't schedule_on_each_cpu while holding
   the hotplug lock because we'll re-take cpus_read_lock and cause
   a deadlock condition with cpu-hotplug.

2) I'm not sure we can do it after the hotplug lock as been dropped,
   at least not safely.  At the very least another hot-plug re-adding
   the node could start.  That just seemed like a bad path.

3) foreign cpu access to the per-cpu values are not atomic with
   respect to in-flight folds on the target cpu.  this_cpu_xchg
   and this_cpu_add are (i believe) only atomic wrt the cpu itself
   (can't be interrupted mid-exchange).

doing it before node_offline() has problems (in-flight folds),
doing it after node_offline() still *technically* carries the same
in-flight fold risk - just narrower (fold has to have started already).

I couldn't convince myself there wasn't still a race, so here we are.

> > +	for_each_possible_cpu(cpu) {
> 
> That's a lot of CPUs
> 

Unfortunately - cpus may have gone offline while the node was offline,
so we legitimately have to visit every *possible* cpu :[

> > +		struct per_cpu_nodestat *p = per_cpu_ptr(pgdat->per_cpu_nodestats, cpu);
> >  
> > -		p = per_cpu_ptr(pgdat->per_cpu_nodestats, cpu);
> > +		for (i = 0; i < NR_VM_NODE_STAT_ITEMS; i++)
> 
> and that's a lot of items.

I am aware :[.  I suppose we could vectorize the collection here on some
archs, but I try to avoid being clever where I can.

> 
> I guess the overall loop count won't be large enough to cause issues,
> but it's large!
> 
> Perhaps there's some simple test we can do on the per_cpu_nodestat to
> avoid the inner loop?  Perhaps might need to add a field for this?

Hadn't considered this, but maybe.  Will take a look.

> 
> btw, "for(int i..." is allowed nowadays.  It'll make this code nicer, IMO.
> 

aye aye o7

> And... Sashiko seems to have found a pre-existing issue:
> 	https://sashiko.dev/#/patchset/20260627202243.758289-1-gourry@gourry.net
> 

Will take a look, thanks!

~Gregory

[PATCH] btrfs: add EM_WARN_ON_ONCE() to dump the extent map on a warning Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new macro EM_WARN_ON_ONCE() to the Btrfs file system, which dumps the contents of an extent map to the kernel log before triggering a warning and stack trace when certain consistency checks fail. The patch addresses a problem where WARN_ON() warnings were not providing enough information about the offending extent maps, leading to crashes in production kernels. The EM_WARN_ON_ONCE() macro is always compiled in, unlike other similar macros that are gated behind configuration options, ensuring that it can provide actionable context even on production systems.

This patch adds a GETFH operation to the create_obj() compound in the NFSv4.1 server tests. The change is made by modifying the environment.py file in the server41tests directory.

Jeff Layton (author) Neutral LLM
The author is addressing a concern about the addition of a new flag to negotiate extensions to directory delegations, specifically that it's part of an RFC proposal and required by the Linux nfs server for non-recall-only delegations.
Show original comment 
This flag has been proposed as part of RFC8881bis. This flag is used to
negotiate extensions to the original directory delegations originally
specified in RFC8881.

In practice, the Linux nfs server requires that the client support this
flag if it's requesting anything other than a recall-only delegation.

Signed-off-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>

+from .st_create_session import create_session
+from .st_open import open_claim4
+from xdrdef.nfs4_const import *
+from xdrdef.nfs4_pack import NFS4Unpacker
+
+from .environment import check, fail, create_obj, use_obj
+from xdrdef.nfs4_type import *
+import nfs_ops
+op = nfs_ops.NFS4ops()
+import nfs4lib
+import threading
+
+zerotime = nfstime4(seconds=0, nseconds=0)
+
+def decode_notify_event(change):
+    """Decode a notify4 into its typed event structure."""
+    mask = nfs4lib.bitmap2list(change.notify_mask)
+    unpacker = NFS4Unpacker(change.notify_vals)
+    if NOTIFY4_REMOVE_ENTRY in mask:
+        return (NOTIFY4_REMOVE_ENTRY, unpacker.unpack_notify_remove4())
+    elif NOTIFY4_ADD_ENTRY in mask:
+        return (NOTIFY4_ADD_ENTRY, unpacker.unpack_notify_add4())
+    elif NOTIFY4_RENAME_ENTRY in mask:
+        return (NOTIFY4_RENAME_ENTRY, unpacker.unpack_notify_rename4())
+    elif NOTIFY4_CHANGE_DIR_ATTRS in mask:
+        return (NOTIFY4_CHANGE_DIR_ATTRS, unpacker.unpack_notify_attr4())
+    return (None, None)
+
+def bitmap4_to_int(bitmap):
+    """Convert a bitmap4 (list of uint32 words) to a single integer."""
+    result = 0
+    for i, word in enumerate(bitmap):
+        result |= word << (32 * i)
+    return result
+
+def _getDirDeleg(t, env, notify_mask, cb):
+    def recall_pre_hook(arg, env):
+        cb.stateid = arg.stateid # NOTE this must be done before set()
+        cb.cred = env.cred.raw_cred
+        cb.got_recall = True
+        env.notify = cb.set # This is called after compound sent to queue
+    def recall_post_hook(arg, env, res):
+        return res
+    def notify_pre_hook(arg, env):
+        cb.stateid = arg.cna_stateid
+        cb.fh = arg.cna_fh
+        cb.changes = arg.cna_changes
+        cb.got_notify = True
+        env.notify = cb.set # This is called after compound sent to queue
+    def notify_post_hook(arg, env, res):
+        return res
+
+    cb.got_recall = False
+    cb.got_notify = False
+
+    c = env.c1
+    sess1 = c.new_client_session(b"%s_1" % env.testname(t))
+    sess1.client.cb_pre_hook(OP_CB_RECALL, recall_pre_hook)
+    sess1.client.cb_post_hook(OP_CB_RECALL, recall_post_hook)
+    sess1.client.cb_pre_hook(OP_CB_NOTIFY, notify_pre_hook)
+    sess1.client.cb_post_hook(OP_CB_NOTIFY, notify_post_hook)
+
+    topdir = c.homedir + [t.code.encode('utf8')]
+    res = create_obj(sess1, topdir)
+    check(res)
+    fh = res.resarray[-1].object
+
+    ops = [ op.putfh(fh), op.get_dir_delegation(False,
+                                                nfs4lib.list2bitmap(notify_mask),
+                                                zerotime, zerotime,
+                                                nfs4lib.list2bitmap([]),
+                                                nfs4lib.list2bitmap([]))]
+    res = sess1.compound(ops)
+    check(res)
+    deleg = res.resarray[-1].gddrnf_resok4.gddr_stateid
+    return (sess1, fh, deleg)
+
+def testDirDelegSimple(t, env):
+    """Test basic dir delegation handout, recall and return
+
+    FLAGS: dirdeleg all
+    CODE: DIRDELEG1
+    """
+    c = env.c1
+    recall = threading.Event()
+    sess1, fh, deleg = _getDirDeleg(t, env, [], recall)
+
+    # new client -- create a file in the dir
+    sess2 = c.new_client_session(b"%s_2" % env.testname(t))
+    claim = open_claim4(CLAIM_NULL, env.testname(t))
+    owner = open_owner4(0, b"owner")
+    how = openflag4(OPEN4_CREATE, createhow4(GUARDED4, {FATTR4_SIZE:0}))
+    open_op = [ op.putfh(fh), op.open(0,
+                                      OPEN4_SHARE_ACCESS_WRITE | OPEN4_SHARE_ACCESS_WANT_NO_DELEG,
+                                      OPEN4_SHARE_DENY_NONE, owner, how, claim), op.getfh() ]
+    slot = sess2.compound_async(open_op)
+    completed = recall.wait(2)
+    env.sleep(.1)
+
+    ops = [ op.putfh(fh), op.delegreturn(deleg) ]
+    res = sess1.compound(ops)
+    check(res)
+
+    # Reap the async open and close any file it created
+    res = sess2.listen(slot)
+    if res.status == NFS4_OK:
+        open_stateid = res.resarray[-2].stateid
+        file_fh = res.resarray[-1].object
+        close_file(sess2, file_fh, stateid=open_stateid)

support. The rest require CB_NOTIFY support. If the server doesn't

+def testDirDelegMkdirRecall(t, env):
+    """Verify mkdir triggers dir delegation recall
+
+    FLAGS: dirdeleg all
+    CODE: DIRDELEG5
+    """
+    c = env.c1
+    recall = threading.Event()
+    sess1, fh, deleg = _getDirDeleg(t, env, [], recall)
+
+    # Create a subdirectory from sess2 -- should trigger recall
+    sess2 = c.new_client_session(b"%s_2" % env.testname(t))
+    create_op = [ op.putfh(fh),
+                  op.create(createtype4(NF4DIR), env.testname(t),
+                            {FATTR4_MODE: 0o755}) ]
+    slot = sess2.compound_async(create_op)
+    completed = recall.wait(2)
+    env.sleep(.1)
+
+    ops = [ op.putfh(fh), op.delegreturn(deleg) ]
+    res = sess1.compound(ops)
+    check(res)

+
+def testDirDelegDuplicate(t, env):
+    """Test that server returns GDD4_UNAVAIL on duplicate GDD4 request
+
+    FLAGS: dirdeleg all
+    CODE: DIRDELEG2
+    """
+    c = env.c1
+    recall = threading.Event()
+    sess1, fh, deleg = _getDirDeleg(t, env, [], recall)
+
+    # get a dir deleg with no notifications
+    ops = [ op.putfh(fh), op.get_dir_delegation(False,
+                                                nfs4lib.list2bitmap([]),
+                                                zerotime, zerotime,
+                                                nfs4lib.list2bitmap([]),
+                                                nfs4lib.list2bitmap([]))]
+    res = sess1.compound(ops)
+    check(res)
+    nfstatus = res.resarray[-1].gddr_res_non_fatal4.gddrnf_status
+    if (nfstatus != GDD4_UNAVAIL):
+        fail("Server replied to duplicate request with %d" % nfstatus)
+
+    ops = [ op.putfh(fh), op.delegreturn(deleg) ]
+    res = sess1.compound(ops)
+    check(res)

     check(res)
+
+def testDirDelegRemoveRecall(t, env):
+    """Verify remove triggers dir delegation recall
+
+    FLAGS: dirdeleg all
+    CODE: DIRDELEG3
+    """
+    c = env.c1
+    recall = threading.Event()
+    sess1, fh, deleg = _getDirDeleg(t, env, [], recall)
+
+    # Create a file from sess1
+    claim = open_claim4(CLAIM_NULL, env.testname(t))
+    owner = open_owner4(0, b"owner")
+    how = openflag4(OPEN4_CREATE, createhow4(GUARDED4, {FATTR4_SIZE:0}))
+    open_op = [ op.putfh(fh), op.open(0,
+                                      OPEN4_SHARE_ACCESS_WRITE | OPEN4_SHARE_ACCESS_WANT_NO_DELEG,
+                                      OPEN4_SHARE_DENY_NONE, owner, how, claim), op.getfh() ]
+    res = sess1.compound(open_op)
+    check(res)
+    open_stateid = res.resarray[-2].stateid
+    file_fh = res.resarray[-1].object
+    close_file(sess1, file_fh, stateid=open_stateid)
+
+    # Remove the file from sess2 -- should trigger recall
+    sess2 = c.new_client_session(b"%s_2" % env.testname(t))
+    remove_op = [ op.putfh(fh), op.remove(env.testname(t)) ]
+    slot = sess2.compound_async(remove_op)
+    completed = recall.wait(2)
+    env.sleep(.1)
+
+    ops = [ op.putfh(fh), op.delegreturn(deleg) ]
+    res = sess1.compound(ops)
+    check(res)

+
+def testDirDelegLinkRecall(t, env):
+    """Verify link triggers dir delegation recall
+
+    FLAGS: dirdeleg all
+    CODE: DIRDELEG6
+    """
+    c = env.c1
+    recall = threading.Event()
+    sess1, fh, deleg = _getDirDeleg(t, env, [], recall)
+
+    # Create a file from sess1
+    claim = open_claim4(CLAIM_NULL, env.testname(t))
+    owner = open_owner4(0, b"owner")
+    how = openflag4(OPEN4_CREATE, createhow4(GUARDED4, {FATTR4_SIZE:0}))
+    open_op = [ op.putfh(fh), op.open(0,
+                                      OPEN4_SHARE_ACCESS_WRITE | OPEN4_SHARE_ACCESS_WANT_NO_DELEG,
+                                      OPEN4_SHARE_DENY_NONE, owner, how, claim), op.getfh() ]
+    res = sess1.compound(open_op)
+    check(res)
+    open_stateid = res.resarray[-2].stateid
+    file_fh = res.resarray[-1].object
+    close_file(sess1, file_fh, stateid=open_stateid)
+
+    # Link the file to a new name from sess2 -- should trigger recall
+    sess2 = c.new_client_session(b"%s_2" % env.testname(t))
+    link_op = [ op.putfh(file_fh), op.savefh(),
+                op.putfh(fh),
+                op.link(b"%s_link" % env.testname(t)) ]
+    slot = sess2.compound_async(link_op)
+    completed = recall.wait(2)
+    env.sleep(.1)
+
+    ops = [ op.putfh(fh), op.delegreturn(deleg) ]
+    res = sess1.compound(ops)
+    check(res)

+def testDirDelegRenameRecall(t, env):
+    """Verify rename triggers dir delegation recall
+
+    FLAGS: dirdeleg all
+    CODE: DIRDELEG4
+    """
+    c = env.c1
+    recall = threading.Event()
+    sess1, fh, deleg = _getDirDeleg(t, env, [], recall)
+
+    # Create a file from sess1
+    claim = open_claim4(CLAIM_NULL, env.testname(t))
+    owner = open_owner4(0, b"owner")
+    how = openflag4(OPEN4_CREATE, createhow4(GUARDED4, {FATTR4_SIZE:0}))
+    open_op = [ op.putfh(fh), op.open(0,
+                                      OPEN4_SHARE_ACCESS_WRITE | OPEN4_SHARE_ACCESS_WANT_NO_DELEG,
+                                      OPEN4_SHARE_DENY_NONE, owner, how, claim), op.getfh() ]
+    res = sess1.compound(open_op)
+    check(res)
+    open_stateid = res.resarray[-2].stateid
+    file_fh = res.resarray[-1].object
+
+    # Rename the file from sess2 -- should trigger recall
+    sess2 = c.new_client_session(b"%s_2" % env.testname(t))
+    rename_op = [ op.putfh(fh), op.savefh(),
+                  op.putfh(fh),
+                  op.rename(env.testname(t), b"%s_2" % env.testname(t)) ]
+    slot = sess2.compound_async(rename_op)
+    completed = recall.wait(2)
+    env.sleep(.1)
+
+    ops = [ op.putfh(fh), op.delegreturn(deleg) ]
+    res = sess1.compound(ops)
+    check(res)
+
+    close_file(sess1, file_fh, stateid=open_stateid)

This patch series fixes bugs in the inter-server copy offload code of the NFS daemon, specifically addressing a publish race condition and use-after-free issues in the cpntf state management.

Last comment 2026-06-26 [PATCH v2] sunrpc: hardcode pool_mode to pernode, remove other modes Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch removes the SVC_POOL_* enum and related code, hardcoding the pool mode to pernode (one pool per NUMA node) as it is considered the best choice today. The percpu mode, which created excessive pools, is removed along with its auto-selection logic. The module parameter and netlink interfaces are preserved for backward compatibility. If pool map allocation fails, service creation now fails instead of silently falling back to a single global pool.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Chuck Lever ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Chuck Lever noted that a server could end up with pools having no threads due to the way svc_set_num_threads() distributes threads across pools, and asked if there's a way for an admin to avoid empty pools on a server running fewer threads than NUMA nodes. He also pointed out that the documentation still presents the four pool modes as distinct, including global mode which is now removed.
Show original comment 
With one pool per node now mandatory, can a server end up with pools
that have no threads?  svc_set_num_threads() spreads nrservs evenly
across the pools:

net/sunrpc/svc.c:svc_set_num_threads() {
	unsigned int base = nrservs / serv->sv_nrpools;
	unsigned int remain = nrservs % serv->sv_nrpools;
	...
}

so when nrservs is smaller than the number of NUMA nodes with CPUs,
base is 0 and the trailing pools get zero threads.

Work is still routed to the per-node pool selected from the running
CPU:

net/sunrpc/svc_xprt.c:svc_xprt_enqueue() {
	pool = svc_pool_for_cpu(xprt->xpt_server);
	...
	lwq_enqueue(&xprt->xpt_ready, &pool->sp_xprts);
	svc_pool_wake_idle_thread(pool);
}

and svc_pool_wake_idle_thread() only consults that one pool's idle
list, with no fallback to a sibling pool.  If a connection lands on a
node whose pool has no threads, does its transport sit on sp_xprts and
never get serviced?

The old global default kept a single pool, so this configuration could
not arise without an explicit pernode/percpu selection.  With global
mode removed, is there a way left for an admin to avoid empty pools on
a server that runs fewer threads than it has NUMA nodes, or should the
thread distribution or the enqueue path guarantee work only reaches a
populated pool?

Separately, the pool_mode knob is preserved but every accepted value
now behaves as pernode.  Documentation/admin-guide/kernel-parameters.txt
still presents the four modes as distinct:

	auto	    the server chooses an appropriate mode
		    automatically using heuristics
	global	    a single global pool contains all CPUs
	percpu	    one pool for each CPU
	pernode	    one pool for each NUMA node (equivalent
		    to global on non-NUMA machines)

and states the option "will affect which CPUs will do NFS serving."
The patch should update this documentation.


-- 
Chuck Lever

Benjamin Coddington ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that optimizing readdir performance for NFS delegations is challenging due to varying syscall patterns in user-space utilities, and suggested re-hydrating the dentry cache from directory page mappings if the delegation is still valid.
Show original comment 
I haven't (shame) thought about READDIR in the context of directory
delegations.

But during my time at Red Hat we worked hard to optimize some readdir
problems and I learned that almost any change we made ended up making
someone's workload regress.  We also found that our performance benchmarks
rarely matched the most common real-world workloads.  We made the mistake of
trying to improve the benchmark which resulted in performance regressions
for real-world users.

Jeff, you've already touched on the core issue regarding fixing this with
bulk GETATTR calls - the kernel doesn't know what syscall pattern the
userspace process is going to use next.  The `ls -l` command and `find` and
friends have complex history and branching logic, they do different lookup
and getattr patterns based on their own goals, and NFS cannot optimize for
any one case.

I think the last time we discussed additional improvements there were some
ideas about teaching the readdir code to respond to fadvise flags, but then
you'd also need to teach the utilities how to use them as well, and those
utilities try to be filesystem-agnostic.

Its a tough problem, and sometimes the simplest thing might be to just use
more directories on NFS.

**coffee!**

.... er - so with directory delegations, can we simply re-hydrate the dentry
cache from the directory page mappings if the delegation is still valid?
Does the directory delegation pin the mapping?  Clearly I need to look at
the code..

Ben

I have also long hoped for a way to cache or speed up "negative"

So if you have your software (e.g. PYTHONPATH) on an NFS share, then

file in another directory etc etc.

Jeff Layton (author) ↳ replying to Benjamin Coddington Neutral LLM
Author identified the root cause of performance issues as inode attributes and attrcache timeouts, explaining that the first pass uses a full-attrs READDIR but subsequent passes rely on delegation and attrcache, leading to GETATTR calls for statx() operations.
Show original comment 
I think the main problem is not the dcache, but the attributes on the
inodes. The first pass uses a full-attrs READDIR (aka readdirplus) and
everything goes reasonably quick.

Then, on the second pass, the client skips checking directory
attributes before trusting the dcache because of the delegation (which
is good), but the inodes those dentries point to have an attrcache
timeout, and we end up doing a GETATTR for each statx() call when a
readdirplus would have been cheaper.

I guess after you see a few statx() calls from the same pid on the same
directory in a short timeframe you could switch to doing READDIR, but
that sounds horrid to get right and keep working.

-- 
Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
View on lore ↗
Signals: explained technical issue, acknowledged complexity
Jeff Layton (author) ↳ replying to Benjamin Coddington Neutral LLM
Author acknowledged that using fsnotify to watch for child attribute changes is a possible solution, but noted it could be chatty and require nfsd to send callbacks in larger chunks.
Show original comment 
Which I think could be done, at least on the Linux server. fsnotify
does support watching for child attribute changes (FS_EVENT_ON_CHILD |
FS_ATTRIB).

That could be very chatty though. We would need to do the work to make
nfsd send callbacks >1 page in order to keep up, I imagine.

Trond Myklebust ↳ replying to Benjamin Coddington Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Trond Myklebust expressed skepticism about the use of NOTIFY4_CHANGE_CHILD_ATTRS in informing clients whether to use READDIR or READDIRPLUS, noting that receiving a notification does not guarantee safety from attribute changes and suggesting an alternative approach.
Show original comment 
Unlike delegations, notifications are asynchronous by design. Upon
reception of a notification, you may deduce that you should revalidate
the attributes on a file. However the absence of that notification is
insufficient to deduce that it is safe not to revalidate those
attributes.

So when it comes to informing the client whether to use READDIR or
READDIRPLUS, I'm sceptical concerning the value of throwing
NOTIFY4_CHANGE_CHILD_ATTRS at the problem.

Trond Myklebust

Last comment 2026-06-23 Re: NFS delegations behavior analysis Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch is related to NFS delegations behavior analysis and appears to be a discussion about tuning heuristics for improving performance, specifically regarding ls-ish workloads that may regress.

7 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Benjamin Coddington ↳ replying to Jeff Layton Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer noted that optimizing readdir performance for NFS delegations is challenging due to varying syscall patterns in user-space utilities, and suggested re-hydrating the dentry cache from directory page mappings if the delegation is still valid.
Show original comment 
I haven't (shame) thought about READDIR in the context of directory
delegations.

But during my time at Red Hat we worked hard to optimize some readdir
problems and I learned that almost any change we made ended up making
someone's workload regress.  We also found that our performance benchmarks
rarely matched the most common real-world workloads.  We made the mistake of
trying to improve the benchmark which resulted in performance regressions
for real-world users.

Jeff, you've already touched on the core issue regarding fixing this with
bulk GETATTR calls - the kernel doesn't know what syscall pattern the
userspace process is going to use next.  The `ls -l` command and `find` and
friends have complex history and branching logic, they do different lookup
and getattr patterns based on their own goals, and NFS cannot optimize for
any one case.

I think the last time we discussed additional improvements there were some
ideas about teaching the readdir code to respond to fadvise flags, but then
you'd also need to teach the utilities how to use them as well, and those
utilities try to be filesystem-agnostic.

Its a tough problem, and sometimes the simplest thing might be to just use
more directories on NFS.

**coffee!**

.... er - so with directory delegations, can we simply re-hydrate the dentry
cache from the directory page mappings if the delegation is still valid?
Does the directory delegation pin the mapping?  Clearly I need to look at
the code..

Ben

Signals: requested changes
Jeff Layton (author) ↳ replying to Benjamin Coddington Neutral LLM
Author identified the root cause of performance issues as inode attributes and attrcache timeouts, explaining that the first pass uses a full-attrs READDIR but subsequent passes rely on delegation and attrcache, leading to GETATTR calls for statx() operations.
Show original comment 
I think the main problem is not the dcache, but the attributes on the
inodes. The first pass uses a full-attrs READDIR (aka readdirplus) and
everything goes reasonably quick.

Then, on the second pass, the client skips checking directory
attributes before trusting the dcache because of the delegation (which
is good), but the inodes those dentries point to have an attrcache
timeout, and we end up doing a GETATTR for each statx() call when a
readdirplus would have been cheaper.

I guess after you see a few statx() calls from the same pid on the same
directory in a short timeframe you could switch to doing READDIR, but
that sounds horrid to get right and keep working.

-- 
Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>

Chuck Lever Positive LLM
Confirmed that the patch addresses a gap in earlier series and is fine as it stands.
Show original comment 
On Mon, 2026-06-22 at 07:57 -0400, Jeff Layton wrote:
> Can nn->nfsd_serv be non-NULL while the NFSv4 state tables are still
> NULL?
> ...
> Would checking nn->nfsd_net_up instead of nn->nfsd_serv be more
> accurate here, since nfsd_net_up is set at the tail of
> nfsd_startup_net() exactly when the state tables become valid?

Yep, that's exactly the gap. The hunk you've quoted is 1/3 of the
earlier "post-shutdown use-after-free" series, which only guarded the
shutdown side of the window. nn->nfsd_serv is also set at service
creation, before nfsd_startup_net() allocates conf_id_hashtbl, so the
startup side was still exposed.

The thread you're replying to is the follow-on that closes it: it
switches all three unlock paths (write_unlock_fs,
nfsd_nl_unlock_filesystem_doit, nfsd_nl_unlock_export_doit) from
nn->nfsd_serv to test_bit(NFSD_NET_UP, &nn->flags) and rewrites the
walkers' Context: notes to match. NFSD_NET_UP is set at the tail of
nfsd_startup_net() and cleared in nfsd_shutdown_net() after the tables
are freed, so it brackets precisely the window you describe.

I assume your comment was meant for 1/3 of that series rather than
this thread... but we've reached the same conclusion, so no action
needed beyond this patch, correct?

-- 
Chuck Lever

  • [10/10] docs: fuse: add io-uring buffer pool and zero-copy documentation Submitted 2026-06-12
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds buffer pools and zero-copy to the fuse io-uring implementation, allowing servers to pass in a managed buffer pool that reduces memory usage requirements and enables future optimizations. The decoupling of ents and buffers also eliminates the need for dedicated payload buffers, reducing memory waste. Additionally, the series introduces zero-copy functionality, which allows direct access to client pages or page cache folios without copying data through an intermediary buffer, requiring CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges and registered buffers.

9 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Joanne Koong (author) Positive LLM
The author simplified the logic for fetching and sending off the next request, removing duplicated code from fuse_uring_send_next_to_ring(), and decoupling request fetching from the send operation.
Show original comment 
Simplify the logic for fetching + sending off the next request.

This gets rid of fuse_uring_send_next_to_ring() which contained
duplicated logic from fuse_uring_send(). This decouples request fetching
from the send operation, which makes the control flow clearer and
reduces unnecessary parameter passing.

2.52.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged fix needed, agreed to restructure
Joanne Koong (author) Positive LLM
The author addressed a concern about the tight coupling between ents and buffers in fuse io-uring, explaining that they have decoupled them by allowing servers to pass in a buffer pool (a contiguous allocation of buffers) that the kernel will use for servicing ents/requests. The server must set the FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL flag during registration and provide the payload buffer pool through the iovec payload in the first sqe. Buffer pool usage is set on a per-ring basis, and the kernel manages buffer selection and recycling through a buffer ring data structure.
Show original comment 
Right now, ents and buffers are tightly coupled in fuse io-uring where
each entry has its own dedicated payload buffer, requiring N buffers for
N entries where each buffer must be large enough to accomodate the
maximum payload size. This is suboptimal as most request types (lookup,
open, release, getattr, etc) require vastly less bytes than the maximum
payload size and some requests (unlink, rmdir, fsync, flush, etc) do not
require payload buffers at all.

Instead of requiring a 1:1 coupling between ents and payload buffers,
allow the server to pass in a buffer pool (a contiguous allocation of
buffers) that the kernel will use as it wishes for servicing
ents/requests. Buffers are now pooled and selected on demand. Entries
only "reserve" a buffer while actively processing a request that
requires a payload buffer. This decoupling reduces the memory usage
requirements needed to use fuse-io-uring. It allows for future
optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption which will allow
non-overlapping regions of a single buffer to be used simultaneously
across multiple ents/requests, and the possibility of multiple buffer
sizes to be supported per ring in the future.

To use this, the server must set the FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL flag during
registration and provide the payload buffer pool through the iovec
payload in the first sqe. The server must set init.buf_size and
init.nr_bufs in the struct fuse_uring_cmd_req it passes to the kernel to
describe the buffer pool configuration. Buffer pool usage is set on a
per-ring basis. All subsequent registration SQEs for the same ring must
pass in consistent flags.

Internally, a buffer ring data structure is used for managing the buffer
pool. The kernel manages buffer selection and recycling through this
ring structure. A payload buffer is selected from the ring on demand if
the request requires a payload buffer and recycled after the request
completes if used.

The contiguous buffer pool passed in is partitioned into individual
buffers according to the buf_size passed in at registration time.

  PAYLOAD BUFFER POOL (contiguous, provided via iov[1] in first sqe):
    |<-------------- payload_size ------------>|
    +--------- --+-----------+-----------+-----+
    |  buf [0]   |  buf [1]  |  buf [2]  | ... |
    |  buf_size  |  buf_size |  buf_size | ... |
    +--------- --+-----------+-----------+-----+

The buffer ring logic is as follows:
select:  buf = bufs[head % nbufs]; head++
recycle: bufs[tail % nbufs] = buf; tail++
empty:   tail == head (no buffers available)
full:    tail - head >= nbufs

Buffer ring request flow
------------------------
|  Kernel                                  |  FUSE daemon
|                                          |
|  [client request arrives]                |
|  >fuse_uring_send()                      |
|    [select payload buf from pool]        |
|    >fuse_uring_select_buffer()           |
|    [copy headers to ent]                 |
|    >copy_header_to_ring()                |
|    [copy payload to selected buf]        |
|    >fuse_uring_copy_to_ring()            |
|    [set buf_id in ent_in_out header]     |
|    >io_uring_cmd_done()                  |
|                                          |  [CQE received]
|                                          |  [read headers]
|                                          |  [read payload from buf_id]
|                                          |  [process request]
|                                          |  [write reply to header]
|                                          |  [write reply payload to
|                                          |    buf]
|                                          |  >io_uring_submit()
|                                          |   COMMIT_AND_FETCH
|  >fuse_uring_commit_fetch()              |
|    >fuse_uring_commit()                  |
|     [copy reply from ring]               |
|     >fuse_uring_recycle_buffer()         |
|    >fuse_uring_get_next_fuse_req()       |

Reviewed-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
Reviewed-by: Baokun Li <libaokun@linux.alibaba.com>
Signed-off-by: Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>

+/*
+ * sqe->addr is a ptr to an iovec array, iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_HEADERS] has the

+ */
+static int fuse_uring_get_iovec_from_sqe(const struct io_uring_sqe *sqe,
+					 struct iovec iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS])
 {
+	struct iovec __user *uiov = u64_to_user_ptr(READ_ONCE(sqe->addr));
+	struct iov_iter iter;
+	ssize_t ret;
+
+	if (sqe->len != FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	/*
+	 * Direction for buffer access will actually be READ and WRITE,
+	 * using write for the import should include READ access as well.
+	 */
+	ret = import_iovec(WRITE, uiov, FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS,
+			   FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS, &iov, &iter);
+	if (ret < 0)
+		return ret;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_bufpool_setup(struct io_uring_cmd *cmd,
+				    struct fuse_ring_queue *queue)
+{
+	const struct fuse_uring_cmd_req *cmd_req =
+		io_uring_sqe128_cmd(cmd->sqe, struct fuse_uring_cmd_req);
+	unsigned int buf_size = READ_ONCE(cmd_req->init.buf_size);
+	unsigned int nr_bufs = READ_ONCE(cmd_req->init.nr_bufs);
+	struct iovec iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_SEGS];
+	uintptr_t payload_addr;
+	size_t payload_size, ring_size;
+	struct fuse_bufpool *pool;
+	unsigned int i;
+	int err;
+
+	if (!buf_size || !nr_bufs)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	err = fuse_uring_get_iovec_from_sqe(cmd->sqe, iov);
+	if (err)
+		return err;
+
+	payload_addr = (uintptr_t)iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_PAYLOAD].iov_base;
+	payload_size = iov[FUSE_URING_IOV_PAYLOAD].iov_len;
+
+	if (payload_size < (uint64_t)buf_size * nr_bufs)
+		return -EINVAL;
+
+	/* create the pool, which is managed as a ring buffer */
+	ring_size = struct_size(pool, bufs, nr_bufs);
+	pool = kzalloc(ring_size, GFP_KERNEL_ACCOUNT);
+	if (!pool)
+		return -ENOMEM;
+
+	/* populate the ring buffer */
+	for (i = 0; i < nr_bufs; i++, payload_addr += buf_size) {
+		struct fuse_bufpool_buf *buf = &pool->bufs[i];
+
+		buf->addr = payload_addr;
+		buf->len = buf_size;
+		buf->id = i;
+	}
+
+	pool->nbufs = nr_bufs;
+	pool->tail = nr_bufs;
+
+	queue->bufpool = pool;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+/*
+ * If the queue is already registered, check that the queue was initialized with
+ * the same init flags set for this FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_REGISTER cmd. all
+ * FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_REGISTER cmds should have the same init fields set on a
+ * per-queue basis.
+ */
+static bool queue_init_flags_consistent(struct fuse_ring_queue *queue,
+					u64 init_flags)
+{
+	bool bufpool = init_flags & FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL;
+
+	return bufpool_enabled(queue) == bufpool;
+}
+

+static bool fuse_uring_req_has_payload(struct fuse_req *req)
+{
+	struct fuse_args *args = req->args;
+
+	return args->in_numargs > 1 || args->out_numargs;
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_select_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent)
+{
+	struct fuse_ring_queue *queue = ent->queue;
+	struct fuse_bufpool *pool = queue->bufpool;
+	struct fuse_bufpool_buf *buf;
+	unsigned int tail = pool->tail, head = pool->head;
+
+	lockdep_assert_held(&queue->lock);
+
+	if (tail == head)
+		return -ENOBUFS;
+
+	buf = &pool->bufs[head % pool->nbufs];
+	pool->head++;
+
+	ent->payload_buf = *buf;
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+static void fuse_uring_recycle_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent)
+{
+	struct fuse_bufpool_buf *ent_payload = &ent->payload_buf;
+	struct fuse_ring_queue *queue = ent->queue;
+	struct fuse_bufpool_buf *buf;
+	struct fuse_bufpool *pool;
+
+	lockdep_assert_held(&queue->lock);
+
+	if (!bufpool_enabled(queue) || !ent_payload->addr)
+		return;
+
+	pool = queue->bufpool;
+
+	/* pool should never be full */
+	WARN_ON_ONCE(pool->tail - pool->head >= pool->nbufs);
+
+	buf = &pool->bufs[(pool->tail) % pool->nbufs];
+	*buf = *ent_payload;
+
+	pool->tail++;
+
+	memset(ent_payload, 0, sizeof(*ent_payload));
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_next_req_update_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+					     struct fuse_req *req)
+{
+	bool buffer_selected;
+	bool has_payload;
+
+	if (!bufpool_enabled(ent->queue))
+		return 0;
+
+	buffer_selected = !!ent->payload_buf.addr;
+	has_payload = fuse_uring_req_has_payload(req);
+
+	if (has_payload && !buffer_selected)
+		return fuse_uring_select_buffer(ent);
+
+	if (!has_payload && buffer_selected)
+		fuse_uring_recycle_buffer(ent);
+
+	return 0;
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_prep_buffer(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+				  struct fuse_req *req)
+{
+	if (!bufpool_enabled(ent->queue))
+		return 0;
+
+	/* no payload to copy, can skip selecting a buffer */
+	if (!fuse_uring_req_has_payload(req))
+		return 0;
+
+	return fuse_uring_select_buffer(ent);
+}

2.52.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: acknowledged a fix, explained their reasoning
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed a concern about the complexity of using registered buffers by explaining that they require setting an init flag and following standard protocol for using registered buffers, which is advertised to userspace via the FUSE_HAS_IO_URING_BUFPOOL init flag. The author provided benchmark results showing around a 10% throughput improvement for direct randreads and a 35% improvement for zero-copy.
Show original comment 
Allow servers to use an io-uring registered buffer as the buffer pool.
When the server registers buffers with io-uring, the pages backing the
buffers are pinned upfront. This eliminates the overhead of
pinning/unpinning user pages and translating virtual addresses per i/o
request. This also allows servers to use the same registered buffers for
subsequent backing store I/O (eg read_fixed/write_fixed), keeping data
in the same pinned pages without additional pinning or mapping overhead
required.

Registered buffers require the buffer pool (FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL) to be
enabled. To use registered buffers, the server needs to register the
payload buffers through io-uring and during registration, set the
FUSE_URING_REGISTERED_BUFFERS init flag (alongside the
FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL flag) in the registration sqe. For all sqes the
server passes in (including the registration ones), it needs to follow
standard protocol for using registered buffers (eg set
sqe->uring_cmd_flags to include IORING_URING_CMD_FIXED, and pass in the
index where the registered buffers reside to sqe->buf_index). Kernel
support for registered buffers is advertised to userspace via the
FUSE_HAS_IO_URING_BUFPOOL init flag (a single umbrella flag).

In benchmarks using passthrough_hp on a high-performance NVMe-backed
system, using registered buffers showed around a 10% throughput
improvement for direct randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2400 MiB/s), a 4%
improvement for direct sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2620 MiB/s),
a 8% improvement for buffered randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2280 MiB/s),
and a 6% improvement for buffered sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to
~2670 MiB/s).

Reviewed-by: Baokun Li <libaokun@linux.alibaba.com>
Signed-off-by: Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>

2.52.0
View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, explanation
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed a concern about the complexity of the zero-copy implementation, explaining that it is necessary to handle both page-backed and non-page-backed arguments in a single request flow. The author provided detailed diagrams of the request flow for both write and read paths, highlighting how pages are registered and unregistered using io_buffer_register_bvec() and io_buffer_unregister(). The author also clarified that some requests may have both page-backed and non-page-backed arguments, which are handled separately.
Show original comment 
Implement zero-copy for fuse io-uring, eliminating memory copies between
userspace, the kernel, and the fuse server for read/write operations.
The server can directly access client pages or page cache folios without
copying data through an intermediary buffer. When a fuse request arrives,
the kernel registers the relevant pages into a sparse slot in the
server's io_uring registered buffer table. The server can then operate
on these pages directly using io-uring fixed buffer operations (eg
read_fixed/write_fixed) and the kernel unregisters these pages when the
request completes. Non-page-backed args (eg op out headers) will go
through the payload buffer as normal.

This requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN and FUSE_URING_REGISTERED_BUFFERS (which
requires using bufpools). Gating on registered buffers keeps the
configuration space small and avoids partially-optimized modes that are
unlikely to be useful in practice. This is gated behind CAP_SYS_ADMIN
because zero-copy allows the server direct access to the client's
underlying pages, rather than operating on an intermediary buffer that
the contents of the client's pages were copied into or on page cache
folios. Kernel support for zero copy is advertised to userspace via the
FUSE_HAS_IO_URING_BUFPOOL init flag (a single umbrella flag).

The request flow for the zero-copy write path (client writes data,
server reads it) is as follows:
=======================================================================
|  Kernel                                   |  FUSE server
|                                           |
|  "write(fd, buf, 1MB)"                    |
|                                           |
|  >sys_write()                             |
|    >fuse_file_write_iter()                |
|      >fuse_send_one()                     |
|        [req->args->in_pages = true]       |
|        [folios hold client write data]    |
|                                           |
|  >fuse_uring_copy_to_ring()               |
|    >copy_header_to_ring(IN_OUT)           |
|      [memcpy fuse_in_header]              |
|    >copy_header_to_ring(OP)               |
|      [memcpy write_in header]             |
|                                           |
|    >fuse_uring_args_to_ring()             |
|      >setup_fuse_copy_state()             |
|        [skip_folio_copy = true]           |
|                                           |
|      >fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy()       |
|        [folio_get for each client folio]  |
|        [build bio_vec array from folios]  |
|        >io_buffer_register_bvec()         |
|          [register pages at
                 ent->zero_copy_index]      |
|        [ent->zero_copied = true]          |
|                                           |
|      >fuse_copy_args()                    |
|        [skip_folio_copy => return 0       |
|         for page arg, skip data copy]     |
|                                           |
|    >copy_header_to_ring(RING_ENT)         |
|      [memcpy ent_in_out]                  |
|    >io_uring_cmd_done()                   |
|                                           |
|                                           | [CQE received]
|                                           |
|                                           | [issue io_uring READ at
|                                           |  ent->zero_copy_index]
|                                           | [reads directly from
|                                           |client's pages (ZERO_COPY)]
|                                           |
|                                           | [write data to backing
|                                           | store]
|                                           |  [submit COMMIT AND FETCH]
|                                           |
|  >fuse_uring_commit_fetch()               |
|    >fuse_uring_commit()                   |
|      >fuse_uring_copy_from_ring()         |
|    >fuse_uring_req_end()                  |
|      >io_buffer_unregister(ent->zero_copy_index) |
|        [unregister sparse buffer]         |
|      >fuse_zero_copy_release()            |
|        [folio_put for each folio]         |
|      [ent->zero_copied = false]           |
|      >fuse_request_end()                  |
|        [wake up client]                   |

The zero-copy read path is analogous.

Some requests may have both page-backed args and non-page-backed args.
For these requests, the page-backed args are zero-copied while the
non-page-backed args are copied to the buffer selected from the buffer
ring:
    zero-copy: pages registered via io_buffer_register_bvec()
    non-page-backed: copied to payload buffer via fuse_copy_args()

For a request whose payload is zero-copied, the
registration/unregistration path looks like:

    register:  fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy()
                 folio_get() for each folio
                 io_buffer_register_bvec(ent->zero_copy_index)

    unregister: fuse_uring_req_end()
                  io_buffer_unregister(ent->zero_copy_index)
                  -> fuse_zero_copy_release() callback
                     folio_put() for each folio

Please note that on abort for in-flight zero-copied requests that have
been sent to userspace, the registered bvec slot remains occupied and
its folios remain pinned until the io-uring ring is destroyed, at which
point io-uring unregisters all buffers and the fuse_zero_copy_release()
callback drops the folio references. Unregistering at teardown would
require operating on the ring context directly, whose validity is hard
to ascertain; this is deemed not worth the complexity for the abort
race, since everything is freed when the ring is torn down.

The throughput improvement from zero-copy depends on how much of the
per-request latency is spent on data copying vs backing I/O. When
backing I/O dominates, the saved memcpy is a negligible fraction of
overall latency. Please also note that for the server to read/write
into the zero-copied pages, the read/write must go through io-uring
as an IORING_OP_READ_FIXED / IORING_OP_WRITE_FIXED operation. If the
server's backing I/O is instantaneous (eg served from cache), the
overhead of the additional io_uring operation may negate the savings
from eliminating the memcpy.

In benchmarks using passthrough_hp on a high-performance NVMe-backed
system, zero-copy showed around a 35% throughput improvement for direct
randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for direct
sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for
buffered randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2470 MiB/s), and a 10% improvement
for buffered sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to ~2750 MiB/s).

The benchmarks were run using:
  fio --name=test_run --ioengine=sync --rw=rand{read,write} --bs=1M
  --size=1G --numjobs=2 --ramp_time=30 --group_reporting=1

Reviewed-by: Jeff Layton <jlayton@kernel.org>
Reviewed-by: Baokun Li <libaokun@linux.alibaba.com>
Signed-off-by: Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com>

+static void fuse_zero_copy_release(void *priv)
+{
+	struct fuse_zero_copy_bvs *zc_bvs = priv;
+	unsigned int i;
+
+	for (i = 0; i < zc_bvs->nr_bvs; i++)
+		folio_put(page_folio(zc_bvs->bvs[i].bv_page));
+
+	kfree(zc_bvs);
+}
+
+static int fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy(struct fuse_ring_ent *ent,
+				       struct fuse_req *req,
+				       unsigned int issue_flags)
+{
+	struct fuse_args_pages *ap;
+	int err, i, ddir = 0;
+	struct fuse_zero_copy_bvs *zc_bvs;
+	struct bio_vec *bvs;
+
+	/* out_pages indicates a read, in_pages indicates a write */
+	if (req->args->out_pages)
+		ddir |= IO_BUF_DEST;
+	if (req->args->in_pages)
+		ddir |= IO_BUF_SOURCE;
+
+	WARN_ON_ONCE(!ddir);
+
+	ap = container_of(req->args, typeof(*ap), args);
+

On Fri, Jun 12, 2026 at 2:06 PM Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> This series adds buffer pools (implemented internally as a buffer ring) and
> zero-copy to fuse io-uring.
>
> Prior to this series, ents and buffers are tightly coupled where each entry
> has its own dedicated payload buffer, requiring N buffers for N entries where
> each buffer must be large enough to accomodate the maximum payload size. This
> is suboptimal as most request types require vastly less bytes than the maximum
> payload size and some requests do not require payload buffers at all.
>
> This series allows servers to pass in a buffer pool (internally managed as a
> buffer ring) that the kernel will use for servicing ents/requests. This
> decoupling reduces the memory usage requirements needed to use fuse-io-uring.
> It allows for future optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption and
> supporting multiple buffer sizes per ring.
>
> This series also allows servers to use io-uring registered buffers as the
> buffer pool which eliminates the overhead of pinning/unpinning user pages and
> translating virtual addreses per i/o request.
>
> This series additionally adds zero copy to fuse io-uring. The server can
> directly access client pages or page cache folios without copying data through
> an intermediary buffer. This requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges and using
> registered buffers. Zero copy (patch 9) has a dependency on io-uring registered
> bvec changes in [1].

Miklos,

The zero-copy patch has a dependency on the bvec patches in Jens's
io-uring tree. Since the merge window opens Monday, it's probably too
tight now to cross-coordinate this for 7.2. What do you prefer for
syncing between the two trees? If Jens stages the patches in a stable
branch, are you fine with pulling that in and then applying the fuse
patches on top?

Patches 7 and 8 (bufpools and registered buffers) don't have this
dependency, but to make the uapi cleaner under one single umbrella
flag, it would be better to co-ship them with the zero-copy patch.

Patches 1-6 are refactoring changes that have been out since October
and have value on their own, independent of the bufpool and zero-copy
work. Could you please pick these up for 7.2 so they don't have to be
carried and repeatedly rebased?

Thanks,
Joanne
View on lore ↗
Signals: clarification, explanation
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
The author addressed Miklos Szeredi's feedback about the buffer pool interface being inconsistent and suggested a new command op (FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_QUEUE_SETUP) to pass in queue-level settings, including the number of reduced queues and bufpool. The author also explained that returning the buffer address directly would require 8 bytes, but using buf_id takes up only 2 bytes and allows for easier incremental buffer consumption in the future.
Show original comment 
On Mon, Jun 15, 2026 at 5:06 AM Miklos Szeredi <miklos@szeredi.hu> wrote:
>
> On Fri, 12 Jun 2026 at 23:07, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
> >
> > Allow servers to use an io-uring registered buffer as the buffer pool.
> > When the server registers buffers with io-uring, the pages backing the
> > buffers are pinned upfront. This eliminates the overhead of
> > pinning/unpinning user pages and translating virtual addresses per i/o
> > request. This also allows servers to use the same registered buffers for
> > subsequent backing store I/O (eg read_fixed/write_fixed), keeping data
> > in the same pinned pages without additional pinning or mapping overhead
> > required.
>
> Are pinned pages accounted and limited?

Yes, pinned pages are accounted and limited by the kernel io-uring
infrastructure (in io_account_mem() in io_uring/rsrc.c) when userspace
registers the buffers in io-uring.

>
> > Registered buffers require the buffer pool (FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL) to be
> > enabled.
>
> Is there any particular reason for this?  Not saying this is bad, just
> wondering...

There's no technical reason it can't but it keeps things simpler (with
the buffer pool, userspace can more optimally register the bufferpool
into one fixed slot since the buffers are contiguous), reduces the
surface area of configurations that need to be tested, and allows the
feature to be advertised under the FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL capability flag
during init instead of needing a separate flag. I think in practice,
servers that would want the benefits from registered buffers would
also want the benefits from buffer pools, so I don't think we're
leaving out a wanted use case.

Thanks,
Joanne

---

On Mon, Jun 15, 2026 at 4:41 AM Miklos Szeredi <miklos@szeredi.hu> wrote:
>
> On Fri, 12 Jun 2026 at 23:07, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> > To use this, the server must set the FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL flag during
> > registration and provide the payload buffer pool through the iovec
> > payload in the first sqe.
>
> I find this interface inconsistent.   Decoupling the buffer(s) from
> the seq's means that the registration should be decoupled as well, no?

Looking at Bernd's reduced queue series [1], I think we'd most likely
want to also pass in the number of reduced queues separate from sqe
registration. I think along the lines of the config sqe Bernd brought
up, it seems cleanest to add a new cmd op
(FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_QUEUE_SETUP) where queue-level settings (eg flags,
number of queues, bufpool, etc) gets passed in. This would have the
nice side effect of doing the queue creation in this op instead of
right now creating the queue lazily when the first sqe gets
registered. On the userspace side, this adds a bit more friction (eg a
two-phase setup where userspace submits the queue setup cmd, reaps its
completion, and reuses that sqe to register an entry), but that seems
like a reasonable tradeoff.

Does this sound ok to you and Bernd?

Thanks,
Joanne

[1] https://lore.kernel.org/fuse-devel/20260529-reduced-nr-ring-queues_3-v5-0-1dc08c2fccf6@bsbernd.com/T/#u

>
> Thanks,
> Miklos
