On Mon, Jun 15, 2026 at 5:33 AM Miklos Szeredi <miklos@szeredi.hu> wrote:
>
> On Fri, 12 Jun 2026 at 23:07, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> > Buffer ring request flow
> > ------------------------
> > |  Kernel                                  |  FUSE daemon
> > |                                          |
> > |  [client request arrives]                |
> > |  >fuse_uring_send()                      |
> > |    [select payload buf from pool]        |
> > |    >fuse_uring_select_buffer()           |
> > |    [copy headers to ent]                 |
> > |    >copy_header_to_ring()                |
> > |    [copy payload to selected buf]        |
> > |    >fuse_uring_copy_to_ring()            |
> > |    [set buf_id in ent_in_out header]     |
> > |    >io_uring_cmd_done()                  |
> > |                                          |  [CQE received]
> > |                                          |  [read headers]
> > |                                          |  [read payload from buf_id]
>
> How does buf_id map to a buffer address?

The server knows the starting address of the bufpool (since it
allocated it) and it knows the size of each buffer in the pool. It can
compute from the buf_id the address corresponding to the buffer.

>
> Wouldn't it be simpler to provide the buffer address directly?

This would have to be returned as part of the struct
fuse_uring_ent_in_out. There's currently 12 bytes that are free to
use. Returning buf_id takes up 2 bytes and I think is better than
using 8 bytes to provide the buffer address. However, returning the
buf id makes adding incremental buffer consumption in the future a bit
trickier (eg the server would need to keep track of the offset into
the previously used buffer and do any alignment rounding up). Maybe a
good compromise is returning a uint32_t offset that corresponds to the
offset in the bufpool? The buf size is a uint32_t, so I think this
would work just as well as providing the buffer address directly.

>
> We also need some uAPI documentation (generally not just for this
> patchset).  What about a new directory:
> Documentation/filesystems/fuse/uapi?

That sounds good. I'll write some uAPI documentation for this and add
it to that new directory.

Thanks,
Joanne

On Mon, Jun 15, 2026 at 6:05 AM Miklos Szeredi <miklos@szeredi.hu> wrote:
>
> On Fri, 12 Jun 2026 at 23:07, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> > The request flow for the zero-copy write path (client writes data,
> > server reads it) is as follows:
> > =======================================================================
> > |  Kernel                                   |  FUSE server
> > |                                           |
> > |  "write(fd, buf, 1MB)"                    |
> > |                                           |
> > |  >sys_write()                             |
> > |    >fuse_file_write_iter()                |
> > |      >fuse_send_one()                     |
> > |        [req->args->in_pages = true]       |
> > |        [folios hold client write data]    |
> > |                                           |
> > |  >fuse_uring_copy_to_ring()               |
> > |    >copy_header_to_ring(IN_OUT)           |
> > |      [memcpy fuse_in_header]              |
> > |    >copy_header_to_ring(OP)               |
> > |      [memcpy write_in header]             |
> > |                                           |
> > |    >fuse_uring_args_to_ring()             |
> > |      >setup_fuse_copy_state()             |
> > |        [skip_folio_copy = true]           |
> > |                                           |
> > |      >fuse_uring_set_up_zero_copy()       |
> > |        [folio_get for each client folio]  |
> > |        [build bio_vec array from folios]  |
> > |        >io_buffer_register_bvec()         |
> > |          [register pages at
> >                  ent->zero_copy_index]      |
> > |        [ent->zero_copied = true]          |
> > |                                           |
> > |      >fuse_copy_args()                    |
> > |        [skip_folio_copy => return 0       |
> > |         for page arg, skip data copy]     |
> > |                                           |
> > |    >copy_header_to_ring(RING_ENT)         |
> > |      [memcpy ent_in_out]                  |
> > |    >io_uring_cmd_done()                   |
> > |                                           |
> > |                                           | [CQE received]
> > |                                           |
> > |                                           | [issue io_uring READ at
> > |                                           |  ent->zero_copy_index]
>
> FIXED_READ takes an address, right?  How does all this work at the
> userspace level?

The userspace FIXED_READ call (eg set up usually through the liburing
api io_uring_prep_read_fixed() [1]) takes a buf_index, not an address.
Userspace would pass the ent->zero_copy_index as the buf index.
ent->zero_copy_index corresponds to the fixed buffer where userspace
told the kernel (at sqe registration time through the "uint16_t
zero_copy_index" init param) to place the zero-copied pages for that
sqe.

Thanks,
Joanne

[1] https://man7.org/linux/man-pages/man3/io_uring_prep_read_fixed.3.html

Signals: clarification, explanation
Joanne Koong (author) Neutral LLM
Author addressed concerns about interface consistency and queue management in the fuse io-uring buffer pools patch series. She acknowledged that decoupling the buffer(s) from the sqe's means registration should be decoupled as well, but disagreed with Miklos' suggestion to add a separate op for bufpool registration. Instead, she proposed using the request type to dispatch metadata ops to non-io queues and read/write ops to io queues, which would simplify queue selection logic.

> >>>>

> >>>>> registration and provide the payload buffer pool through the iovec
> >>>>> payload in the first sqe.
> >>>>
> >>>> I find this interface inconsistent.   Decoupling the buffer(s) from
> >>>> the seq's means that the registration should be decoupled as well, no?
> >>>
> >>> Looking at Bernd's reduced queue series [1], I think we'd most likely
> >>> want to also pass in the number of reduced queues separate from sqe
> >>> registration. I think along the lines of the config sqe Bernd brought
> >>> up, it seems cleanest to add a new cmd op
> >>> (FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_QUEUE_SETUP) where queue-level settings (eg flags,
> >>> number of queues, bufpool, etc) gets passed in.
> >>
> >> Why not a separate op for bufpool registration, so it can be done
> >> independently of the setup, possibly at some later time?
> >
> > I'm not really convinced there's a compelling use case for needing
> > multiple bufpools in one queue. This was the discussion with Bernd in
> > [1] about this. In my opinion, incremental buffer consumption and
> > allowing different queues to support different payload sizes would
> > address the use cases of multiple bufpools within one queue, but would
> > be simpler.
>
> To be honest, I have no idea how to implement queues with different IO
> sizes. What would be the logic to select a queue? And what is if we

With the separation between non-IO queues and IO-queues, I think we
could just look at the request type, and dispatch accordingly so that
the metadata ops go to the non-io queues and the read/write ops go to
the io queues. I think the dispatch logic would be otherwise the same
for choosing which particular queue within the non-io or io queues.

> start with an single max-IO buf and a single queue (per numa) and then
> scale up based on need? Let's say we have:
>
> Start 1 queue (per numa maybe), one max payload buf in that queue and
> allow to scale up to max nr CPUs for the cpu to queue-id mapping. But
> now additionally we get for the same CPU
>
> - multiple queues of the same buf size
> - multiple queues of different buf sizes
>

I'm not seeing why this would be an issue. For multiple queues of the
same buffer size, it would just be normal queue selection and for
different buffer sizes, we'd jjust have two pools of queues (non-io
and io) and select based on request type first which pool then apply
normal queue selection within that pool. I think if a server has needs
for multiple categories of buffer sizes beyond just non-io vs io,
having multiple bufpools per queue makes more sense than dedicated
queues to the specific categories of the different buffer sizes, but I
doubt this level of granularity would be needed.

>
> Also, every queue has its own ring, syscall aggregation is not given
> anymore.

Syscall aggregation still applies within each ring, but I don't think
sysclal aggregation is really a big factor given that libfuse and the
server handles requests as they arrive and respond as soon as the
request is handled, instead of batching multiple responses before
submitting; on the dispatch side, requests are sent one by one as they
come in rather than batched, so there's no aggregation to lose.

Thanks,
Joanne

> >>>>>

> >>>>> (FUSE_IO_URING_CMD_QUEUE_SETUP) where queue-level settings (eg flags,

> >>>>
> >>>> Why not a separate op for bufpool registration, so it can be done
> >>>> independently of the setup, possibly at some later time?
> >>>
> >>> I'm not really convinced there's a compelling use case for needing
> >>> multiple bufpools in one queue. This was the discussion with Bernd in
> >>> [1] about this. In my opinion, incremental buffer consumption and
> >>> allowing different queues to support different payload sizes would
> >>> address the use cases of multiple bufpools within one queue, but would
> >>> be simpler.
> >>
> >> To be honest, I have no idea how to implement queues with different IO
> >> sizes. What would be the logic to select a queue? And what is if we
> >
> > With the separation between non-IO queues and IO-queues, I think we
> > could just look at the request type, and dispatch accordingly so that
> > the metadata ops go to the non-io queues and the read/write ops go to
> > the io queues. I think the dispatch logic would be otherwise the same
> > for choosing which particular queue within the non-io or io queues.
> >
> >> start with an single max-IO buf and a single queue (per numa) and then
> >> scale up based on need? Let's say we have:
> >>
> >> Start 1 queue (per numa maybe), one max payload buf in that queue and
> >> allow to scale up to max nr CPUs for the cpu to queue-id mapping. But
> >> now additionally we get for the same CPU
> >>
> >> - multiple queues of the same buf size
> >> - multiple queues of different buf sizes
> >>
> >
> > I'm not seeing why this would be an issue. For multiple queues of the
> > same buffer size, it would just be normal queue selection and for
> > different buffer sizes, we'd jjust have two pools of queues (non-io
> > and io) and select based on request type first which pool then apply
> > normal queue selection within that pool. I think if a server has needs
> > for multiple categories of buffer sizes beyond just non-io vs io,
> > having multiple bufpools per queue makes more sense than dedicated
> > queues to the specific categories of the different buffer sizes, but I
> > doubt this level of granularity would be needed.
>
> I'm pretty much lost. It is a 3 dimensional matrix, made of cpus,
> buffer-size and buffers. I really want to see a logic how you figured
> which queue has free buffers.

We already have logic for this (done in your reduced queue series). I
think we could just reuse that logic. A basic implementation would be
having a separate bitmap for non-IO queues vs IO queues, and then once
you figure out if the request is an IO or non-IO request, the rest of
the dispatch logic would be the same for choosing a queue from either
the non-IO queue pool or the IO queue pool. If you're concerned about
dispatch complexity, I think separate bufpools per queue ends up being
more complicated.

>
> >
> >>
> >> Also, every queue has its own ring, syscall aggregation is not given
> >> anymore.
> >
> > Syscall aggregation still applies within each ring, but I don't think
> > sysclal aggregation is really a big factor given that libfuse and the
> > server handles requests as they arrive and respond as soon as the
> > request is handled, instead of batching multiple responses before
> > submitting; on the dispatch side, requests are sent one by one as they
> > come in rather than batched, so there's no aggregation to lose.
>
> Depends, if fuse server starts with 1 buffer per queue and slowly scales
> up, you basically get exactly one buffer per queue. That is the end of
> aggregation.
>

I really don't think servers will start with 1 buffer per queue.

Happy to discuss this together in the call later today.

Thanks,
Joanne

On Thu, Jun 18, 2026 at 1:37 AM Miklos Szeredi <miklos@szeredi.hu> wrote:
>
> On Thu, 18 Jun 2026 at 09:03, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> > imo the main benefit is for incremental buffer consumption (allowing
> > non-overlapping parts of a buffer to be used by multiple entries
> > concurrently). To recycle back a buffer that is used by multiple
> > entries, it would require all requests to have been completed but I
> > don't think in practice this is a bottleneck with reasonably sized
> > buffer pools across queues. imo incremental buffer consumption scales
> > more dynamically with runtime workloads than preallocating fixed
> > buffers of different sizes across different queues trying to
> > anticipate / guess workload needs. Overprovisioning small buffers
> > wastes memory if those requests never appear, while splitting the
> > memory budget towards smaller pools reduces capacity for concurrent
> > large requests, and workload patterns might need either at different
> > times.
>
> Hmm, so why partition this memory into buffers up front?
>
> We could just as well say that managing that chunk of  memory is up to
> the kernel.  This would simplify the interface: the buffer size is
> redundant, since it's the same as the max I/O size, no?

That's a good point, especially since we can just return back offset
into the bufpools instead of buffer id. That's a much cleaner
interface. Though I guess if we want to be forwards-compatible with
having the option of multiple bufpools per queue in the future, we'd
have to add bufsize to the uapi here now. Do you think we should just
keep it as is then?

>
> > I think we should still make the design for bufpools
> > forward-compatible with supporting multiple bufpools if a use case
> > comes up in the future,
>
> Yeah, and separating buffer registration into a separate op would be
> one step in that direction.   It doesn't have to support multiple
> bufpools from the start, but splitting out separate functions during
> initialization is a good idea regardless.

I'm not sure I agree that splitting it out would be a good idea if we
don't ever end up supporting multiple bufpools (imo, having this in
QUEUE_SETUP would logically make sense and be more ergonomic) but I'm
happy to make this change.

>
> > From my perspective, the use case I see for queues supporting
> > different payload sizes is for separating IO requests from non-IO
> > requests (eg lookups, getattrs) onto different queues so that non-IO
> > requests don't get stuck waiting behind larger IO requests.
>
> Note, "queue" could mean two things here:
>
>  - fuse's waiting list
>  - cqe's on the ring
>
> Currently there's 1:1 relation between those, but that's not mandatory
> going forward.  So we could have multiple waiting queues for different
> types of requests, but a single uring for sending them to the server.

Even with separate waiting queues and the kernel prioritizing sending
metadata requests before io, isn't there the issue that if the
requests/entries on the ring are all IO requests and then a storm of
metadata ops come in, they're still backed up waiting for the IO
requests to be finished? Or am I missing something?

On the userspace side, I think they'd need a threadpool which would
add extra context-switching overhead and would have to reserve a few
threads in that pool just for non-IO requests, and then have a way to
inspect requests in the ring to prioritize the non-IO ones, which
seems nontrivial. But maybe there's some simpler way to do this that
I'm not seeing.

Thanks,
Joanne

> > > buffers of different sizes across different queues trying to
> > > anticipate / guess workload needs. Overprovisioning small buffers
> > > wastes memory if those requests never appear, while splitting the
> > > memory budget towards smaller pools reduces capacity for concurrent
> > > large requests, and workload patterns might need either at different
> > > times.
> >

Ignore the last two sentences please, I didn't think through your
original message.

I like your idea a lot about just passing a chunk of memory to the
kernel and offloading the management logic entirely to the kernel.
Maybe I'm missing something obvious, but why would multiple pools per
queue still be needed? If the server needs more capacity, why not just
have the server spin up a new queue? That would have the included
benefit of adding more concurrency, and dynamically adding more queues
is a feature that would be added to fuse uring regardless. For
multipools in one queue, the pools would be discontiguous anyways,
which wouldn't help with fragmentation. I don't think adding a queue
incurs that much memory or cpu overhead, and imo the scenarios where a
server would want to just add more capacity but not parallelism are
uncommon.

>
> >
> > > I think we should still make the design for bufpools
> > > forward-compatible with supporting multiple bufpools if a use case
> > > comes up in the future,
> >
> > Yeah, and separating buffer registration into a separate op would be
> > one step in that direction.   It doesn't have to support multiple
> > bufpools from the start, but splitting out separate functions during
> > initialization is a good idea regardless.
>
> I'm not sure I agree that splitting it out would be a good idea if we
> don't ever end up supporting multiple bufpools (imo, having this in
> QUEUE_SETUP would logically make sense and be more ergonomic) but I'm
> happy to make this change.
>
> >
> > > From my perspective, the use case I see for queues supporting
> > > different payload sizes is for separating IO requests from non-IO
> > > requests (eg lookups, getattrs) onto different queues so that non-IO
> > > requests don't get stuck waiting behind larger IO requests.
> >
> > Note, "queue" could mean two things here:
> >
> >  - fuse's waiting list
> >  - cqe's on the ring
> >
> > Currently there's 1:1 relation between those, but that's not mandatory
> > going forward.  So we could have multiple waiting queues for different
> > types of requests, but a single uring for sending them to the server.
>
> Even with separate waiting queues and the kernel prioritizing sending
> metadata requests before io, isn't there the issue that if the
> requests/entries on the ring are all IO requests and then a storm of
> metadata ops come in, they're still backed up waiting for the IO
> requests to be finished? Or am I missing something?

Thinking about this some more, I think the kernel could just
pre-reserve some ring entries specifically for metadata ops / non-IO
requests. That solves it on the kernel side but the userspace side
would still need a threadpool of some sort if its backend IO blocks
its queue thread.

Thanks,
Joanne

On 6/12/26 3:46 PM, Joanne Koong wrote:
> On Fri, Jun 12, 2026 at 2:06?PM Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>>
>> This series adds buffer pools (implemented internally as a buffer ring) and
>> zero-copy to fuse io-uring.
>>
>> Prior to this series, ents and buffers are tightly coupled where each entry
>> has its own dedicated payload buffer, requiring N buffers for N entries where
>> each buffer must be large enough to accomodate the maximum payload size. This
>> is suboptimal as most request types require vastly less bytes than the maximum
>> payload size and some requests do not require payload buffers at all.
>>
>> This series allows servers to pass in a buffer pool (internally managed as a
>> buffer ring) that the kernel will use for servicing ents/requests. This
>> decoupling reduces the memory usage requirements needed to use fuse-io-uring.
>> It allows for future optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption and
>> supporting multiple buffer sizes per ring.
>>
>> This series also allows servers to use io-uring registered buffers as the
>> buffer pool which eliminates the overhead of pinning/unpinning user pages and
>> translating virtual addreses per i/o request.
>>
>> This series additionally adds zero copy to fuse io-uring. The server can
>> directly access client pages or page cache folios without copying data through
>> an intermediary buffer. This requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges and using
>> registered buffers. Zero copy (patch 9) has a dependency on io-uring registered
>> bvec changes in [1].
> 
> Miklos,
>

> patches on top?
> 
> Patches 7 and 8 (bufpools and registered buffers) don't have this
> dependency, but to make the uapi cleaner under one single umbrella
> flag, it would be better to co-ship them with the zero-copy patch.
> 
> Patches 1-6 are refactoring changes that have been out since October

the 4 io_uring prep patches are staged, they are in my

Since there will be conflicts with either the io_uring branches or block

On Fri, 12 Jun 2026 at 23:07, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> Allow servers to use an io-uring registered buffer as the buffer pool.
> When the server registers buffers with io-uring, the pages backing the
> buffers are pinned upfront. This eliminates the overhead of
> pinning/unpinning user pages and translating virtual addresses per i/o
> request. This also allows servers to use the same registered buffers for
> subsequent backing store I/O (eg read_fixed/write_fixed), keeping data
> in the same pinned pages without additional pinning or mapping overhead
> required.

Are pinned pages accounted and limited?

> Registered buffers require the buffer pool (FUSE_URING_BUFPOOL) to be
> enabled.

Is there any particular reason for this?  Not saying this is bad, just
wondering...

On Fri, 12 Jun 2026 at 23:06, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:
>
> This series adds buffer pools (implemented internally as a buffer ring) and
> zero-copy to fuse io-uring.
>
> Prior to this series, ents and buffers are tightly coupled where each entry
> has its own dedicated payload buffer, requiring N buffers for N entries where
> each buffer must be large enough to accomodate the maximum payload size. This
> is suboptimal as most request types require vastly less bytes than the maximum
> payload size and some requests do not require payload buffers at all.
>
> This series allows servers to pass in a buffer pool (internally managed as a
> buffer ring) that the kernel will use for servicing ents/requests. This
> decoupling reduces the memory usage requirements needed to use fuse-io-uring.
> It allows for future optimizations such as incremental buffer consumption and
> supporting multiple buffer sizes per ring.
>
> This series also allows servers to use io-uring registered buffers as the
> buffer pool which eliminates the overhead of pinning/unpinning user pages and
> translating virtual addreses per i/o request.
>
> This series additionally adds zero copy to fuse io-uring. The server can
> directly access client pages or page cache folios without copying data through
> an intermediary buffer. This requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN privileges and using
> registered buffers. Zero copy (patch 9) has a dependency on io-uring registered
> bvec changes in [1].

Overall I think this series is good, the only part notably lacking is
concise uAPI documentation.

I'd even suggest dropping all internal implementation details from the
documentation that was provided, since adding those details to the
commit messages (which you did) should be sufficient.

Thanks,
Miklos


>
> This series is on top of commit f21c8b1a3b740 in for-next.
>
> The throughput improvements from registered buffers and zero-copy depends on
> how much of the server's per-request latency is spent on data copying vs
> backing I/O. When backing I/O dominates, the saved memcpy is a negligible
> fraction of overall latency. Please also note that for the server to
> read/write into the zero-copied pages, the read/write must go through io-uring
> as an IORING_OP_READ_FIXED / IORING_OP_WRITE_FIXED operation. If the
> server's backing I/O is instantaneous (eg served from cache), the
> overhead of the additional io_uring operation may negate the savings
> from eliminating the memcpy.
>
> In benchmarks using passthrough_hp on a high-performance NVMe-backed
> system, using registered buffers showed around a 10% throughput improvement
> for direct randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2400 MiB/s), a 4% improvement for direct
> sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2620 MiB/s), a 8% improvement for buffered
> randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2280 MiB/s), and a 6% improvement for buffered
> sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to ~2670 MiB/s).
>
> Zero-copy showed around a 35% throughput improvement for direct
> randreads (~2150 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for direct
> sequential reads (~2510 MiB/s to ~2900 MiB/s), a 15% improvement for
> buffered randreads (~2100 MiB/s to ~2470 MiB/s), and a 10% improvement
> for buffered sequential reads (~2500 MiB/s to ~2750 MiB/s). I didn't see
> enough of a clear improvement for writes due to write latency being I/O
> dominated.
>
> The benchmarks were run using:
>   fio --name=test_run --ioengine=sync --rw=rand{read,write} --bs=1M
>   --size=1G --numjobs=2 --ramp_time=30 --group_reporting=1
>
> The libfuse changes can be found in [2]. To test the server, run:
> sudo ~/libfuse/build/example/passthrough_hp ~/src ~/mounts/tmp
> --nopassthrough -o io_uring_zero_copy -o io_uring_q_depth=8
> Once this series is merged, the libfuse changes will be tidied up and
> submitted upstream.
>
> Thanks,
> Joanne
>
> [1] https://lore.kernel.org/io-uring/20260612184840.4058966-1-joannelkoong@gmail.com/T/#t
> [2] https://github.com/joannekoong/libfuse/commits/zero_copy_v4/

On Tue, 16 Jun 2026 at 07:46, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:

> The userspace FIXED_READ call (eg set up usually through the liburing
> api io_uring_prep_read_fixed() [1]) takes a buf_index, not an address.

AFAICS buf_index is required, but is not sufficient.  The fixed ops
require an address as well (to figure out the offset within the
registered buffer).

> Userspace would pass the ent->zero_copy_index as the buf index.
> ent->zero_copy_index corresponds to the fixed buffer where userspace
> told the kernel (at sqe registration time through the "uint16_t
> zero_copy_index" init param) to place the zero-copied pages for that
> sqe.

Okay so there are two buffers per sqe, one which is corresponds to a
real userspace address, and a zero copy one that is (by definition)
kernel-only and doesn't actually have an address.

Passing a NULL address to the fixed op seems okay in this case, but
that means the offset within the buffer is always zero.

Or am I misunderstanding something?

Thanks,
Miklos

On Tue, 16 Jun 2026 at 07:38, Joanne Koong <joannelkoong@gmail.com> wrote:

> The server knows the starting address of the bufpool (since it
> allocated it) and it knows the size of each buffer in the pool. It can
> compute from the buf_id the address corresponding to the buffer.

But which buffer?   The interface is supposed to handle multiple
pools, in which case the server needs to find the pool and the buffer
within the pool.

FIXED ops also require buf_index.  Things are getting complicated...

> This would have to be returned as part of the struct
> fuse_uring_ent_in_out. There's currently 12 bytes that are free to
> use.

Does the 12 bytes limit come from the cqe?

> Returning buf_id takes up 2 bytes and I think is better than
> using 8 bytes to provide the buffer address. However, returning the
> buf id makes adding incremental buffer consumption in the future a bit
> trickier (eg the server would need to keep track of the offset into
> the previously used buffer and do any alignment rounding up). Maybe a
> good compromise is returning a uint32_t offset that corresponds to the
> offset in the bufpool? The buf size is a uint32_t, so I think this
> would work just as well as providing the buffer address directly.

Yeah, offset sounds good, but still need to select between the pools.
So should that be the "pool index" that would be the same as
"buf_index" for registered buffers?

Miklos

[PATCH v1] fuse: don't clear folio uptodate on writethrough errors Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch prevents the clearing of a folio's uptodate flag in case of write-through errors on FUSE filesystems. This is necessary to maintain the invariant that non-uptodate folios must not be visible to userspace, which could lead to potential information leaks. The change ensures that if an application writes data and the server fails to persist it or only services a short write, the page cache folio will retain the original data instead of reverting to the server's contents on subsequent reads.

Last comment 2026-06-24 [PATCH v1] fuse: enable large folios Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch enables large folios for the FUSE file system by capping the maximum order at the largest request that can be issued to a fuse device, ensuring that each folio fits within a single request. This change allows the allocator to fall back to smaller folios under memory pressure. The patch includes benchmark results showing significant performance improvements in various workloads, including sequential reads and writes, with some cases seeing up to 141% increase in throughput.

Joanne Koong (author) ↳ replying to Horst Birthelmer Neutral LLM

Show original comment 
Hi Horst,

Those results are with strictlimiting on. After commit 494d2f508883
('fuse: use default writeback accounting') [1], I didn't see any
performance regressions anymore with large folios + strictlimiting on.
More information on why that commit fixed the issue is in [2].

When I ran the benchmarks last week with strictlimiting off, I saw roughly:
    tmpfs:
      seq,  128k    1174 -> 1648 MiB/s    +40%
      seq,  1M      1261 -> 1845 MiB/s    +46%
      rand, 128k    1148 -> 1638 MiB/s    +43%
      rand, 1M      1273 -> 2065 MiB/s    +62%

    xfs on NVMe:
      seq,  128k     621 ->  740 MiB/s    +19%
      seq,  1M       649 ->  776 MiB/s    +20%
      rand, 128k    1020 -> 1515 MiB/s    +49%
      rand, 1M      1125 -> 1895 MiB/s    +68%

Strict limiting on actually had better performance here, which I think
is because with the small dirty limit, the dirtying and the writeback
happen in parallel instead of more dirty pages accumulating and then
writeback getting kicked off. Because the backing device is so fast,
it didn't cost throughput for the dirtying and the writeback to happen
concurrently. The benchmarks were run with fsync, so everything had to
be flushed before the fio run returned. If the backing device was slow
and writes were bursty and fsync wasn't enforced, I think there'd
probably be better performance with strictlimiting off than on, since
the writer would be throttled to the speed of the backing device with
strictlimtiing on.

---

Are you seeing this on your system with strictlimiting on or off? Is
that with commit 494d2f508883 in your tree? What test and server were
you running? Do you know what the speed of the backing device is?

Thanks,
Joanne

Matthew Wilcox ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Needs Work LLM
Identified a double-put bug and expressed concerns that clearing the uptodate flag can lead to MM sanity checks bringing down the machine. Suggested removing calls to clear uptodate in all filesystems.
Show original comment 
On Tue, Jun 23, 2026 at 01:28:42PM -0700, Joanne Koong wrote:
> +++ b/fs/fuse/file.c
> @@ -1250,13 +1250,13 @@ static ssize_t fuse_send_write_pages(struct fuse_io_args *ia,
>  		struct folio *folio = ap->folios[i];
>  
>  		if (err) {
> -			folio_clear_uptodate(folio);
> +			iomap_folio_clear_uptodate(folio);
>  		} else {
>  			if (count >= folio_size(folio) - offset)
>  				count -= folio_size(folio) - offset;
>  			else {
>  				if (short_write)
> -					folio_clear_uptodate(folio);
> +					iomap_folio_clear_uptodate(folio);
>  				count = 0;

I believe this to be a bug.  I talked about this a bit as LSFMM, but the
fundamental problem is that the folio uptodate flag DOES NOT BELONG TO THE
FILESYSTEM (unlike the buffer_head uptodate flag, which definitely does).

The MM has a number of sanity checks that make sure we haven't
done something awful like mapping a !uptodate folio into userspace.
That would potentially leak confidential information to userspace.
So when a filesystem clears the uptodate flag, the MM can come along
and bring down the whole machine because the mapped folio is now !uptodate.

I know we have a terrible writeback error handling story.  But I think
the right thing to do is just take out the calls to clear uptodate.
In all filesystems.  Darrick took it out of iomap in e9c3a8e820ed but
it's time to purge it everywhere.
Signals: bug, concerns about MM sanity checks
Last comment 2026-06-26 [RFC PATCH v1 0/3] iomap: convert to in-iter ->iomap_next() model 3 patches
Show 3 individual patches
  • [1/3] iomap: add ->iomap_next() and iomap_process() helper Neutral Last comment 2026-06-26 Christoph Hellwig Joanne Koong (author)
  • [2/3] xfs: convert read and buffered write iomap ops to ->iomap_next()
  • [3/3] xfs: example of devirtualizing buffered write iomap callbacks Neutral Last comment 2026-06-25 Christoph Hellwig Joanne Koong (author)
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series converts the iomap code to use an in-iter model, where a single callback function is used for both beginning and ending I/O operations. The conversion involves merging two existing functions into one and adding a helper function to process I/O operations. An example of devirtualizing the callbacks on a hot path is also provided to avoid indirect calls through struct iomap_ops. The patch series raises questions about whether this conversion is worth it, given that indirect calls are cheap on modern hardware.

Gao Xiang ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Gao Xiang noted that the patch introduces a potential deadlock scenario where iomap_next() is called while holding the folio lock, and requested that the lock be dropped before calling try_to_unmap(). reviewer noted that the patch does not fully align with their original in-iter direction and provided a specific example of the design model difference they are concerned about Reviewer Gao Xiang noted that the recent iomap implementation is still callback-based and requires arranging mapping logic into unique callbacks, which can be inflexible for filesystems needing additional information. He suggested a high-level fs helper library to wrap up page cache management, IO management, and other operations, allowing for more flexibility in handling different types of I/O.
Show original comment 
Hi Joanne,

On 2026/6/26 05:33, Joanne Koong wrote:

---

Thanks for the reply.

I partially agree since it's much closer, but what I meant is
the design model difference as below.

---

If you take a look at the initial commit of iomap ae259a9c8593
("fs: introduce iomap infrastructure"), the starting point was
to provide `struct iomap_ops` (two callbacks) to get the filesystem
extent mapping and unique entrypoints: iomap_file_buffered_write(),
iomap_page_mkwrite() for buffer writes (write and mmap write).

And then iomap applies to DAX, direct I/O, writeback, buffer read,
etc.  basically for block-based filesystems, and that is why
`struct iomap_ops` is always _one important part of iomap core_
until FUSE adapted.


But if we take a look at the overall requirement, I think what we
need is to provide an easy-use high-level fs helper library to
wrap up the following stuffs:

  1. page cache management (mainly sub-page tracking) and DAX pages;

  2. IO management (buffer I/O, direct I/O, DAX access);

  3. others (like fiemap, bmap, lseek, etc.)


For example, most block filesystems could use them of all, and for
FUSE and networking filesystem, basically they only need 1 and
maybe partial 3.

The recent implementation (including ->iomap_next()), is still
a callback-based model, the main loop is inside `fs/iomap` itself,
and you have to:

  - still arrange the mapping logic into an unique callback, which
is .iomap_next() now, if the mapping logic needs more information
rather than `struct inode *`, they have to add private pointer
somewhere.

  - Take a recent example (buffered read refactoring), need
    `struct iomap_read_folio_ctx` for FUSE and maybe other future
    networking filesystems to callback into their filesystem to
    provide new I/O contexts and submit I/Os.

But actually take a simple example of iomap_read_folio, you:
could change into (just a very rough sketch):

  xxxfs_read_folio {
    struct xxxxfs_private priv;

    iomap_iter(&iter) {   /// it could be a macro
        if (iomap_iter_outofrange(&iter))
		xxxfs_map_extent(&iter, &priv);

        // to maintain the page cache <-> struct iomap_folio_state
        // and handle some type of mapping
        if (iomap_read_folio_iter(&iter) == NEED_NEW_IO_CONTEXT) {
            xxxfs_new_io (&iter, &priv);
        }
    }
    if (xxxfs_if_any_available_io(&iter, &priv))
	xxxfs_submit_io(&iter, &priv);
}

In this model, I really think `struct iomap_read_folio_ctx` and
similar doesn't need any more (another set of callback avoidness),
and both xxxfs_map_extent() and xxxfs_new_io()/ xxxfs_submit_io()
can pass in their per-fs private without need to bother
the iomap-core to add various `private` pointers.

and in the iomap_iter() loop you can omit/change/add any part if
it doesn't fulfill to the filesystem timing.

As for "a lot of duplicate code", I think with proper new designs,
the common part could be wrapped up into macros (for example,
for bdev filesystems, xxxfs_new_io() and xxxfs_submit_io() calls
can be wrapped up into macros, and only leave:

	if (iomap_iter_outofrange(&iter))
		xxxfs_map_extent(&iter, &priv);"

can be customized for each fs as the common macro helpers.

It's just my rough thought, as I said in the previous year, I
still don't have a strong tendency but it's my suggestion over
these years.

Anyway since you're actively working/changing on iomap, so
it's fine to just respect your ideas/efforts if you don't
think it's worth, but as a record of my thoughts here again.

Thanks,
Gao Xiang
View on lore ↗
Signals: potential deadlock, requested changes, partial agreement

He requested a proof-of-concept from Joanne Koong to gauge interest.
Show original comment 
This does sound like a lot of work, and I'm not a 100% sold on
the benefits, although getting rid of callbacks would be nice.

I'd be tempted to say that as a first step we should move the the
single callback, as a second stop (after converting everyone to

to add queues, unless I see that that ring threads occupy all of the core.

here it makes sense to have a very low buffer usage and to increase it

Re: [PATCH v2 0/2] iomap: add helper to keep uptodate bitmap in sync Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Last comment 2026-06-26 Re: [RFC PATCH v1 0/3] iomap: convert to in-iter ->iomap_next() model Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series converts the iomap code to use an in-iter model, where a single callback function is used for both beginning and ending I/O operations. The conversion involves merging two existing functions into one and adding a helper function to process I/O operations. An example of devirtualizing the callbacks on a hot path is also provided to avoid indirect calls through struct iomap_ops. The patch series raises questions about whether this conversion is worth it, given that indirect calls are cheap on modern hardware.

4 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Gao Xiang ↳ replying to Joanne Koong Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Gao Xiang noted that the patch introduces a potential deadlock scenario where iomap_next() is called while holding the folio lock, and requested that the lock be dropped before calling try_to_unmap(). reviewer noted that the patch does not fully align with their original in-iter direction and provided a specific example of the design model difference they are concerned about Reviewer Gao Xiang noted that the recent iomap implementation is still callback-based and requires arranging mapping logic into unique callbacks, which can be inflexible for filesystems needing additional information. He suggested a high-level fs helper library to wrap up page cache management, IO management, and other operations, allowing for more flexibility in handling different types of I/O.
Show original comment 
Hi Joanne,

On 2026/6/26 05:33, Joanne Koong wrote:

---

Thanks for the reply.

I partially agree since it's much closer, but what I meant is
the design model difference as below.

---

If you take a look at the initial commit of iomap ae259a9c8593
("fs: introduce iomap infrastructure"), the starting point was
to provide `struct iomap_ops` (two callbacks) to get the filesystem
extent mapping and unique entrypoints: iomap_file_buffered_write(),
iomap_page_mkwrite() for buffer writes (write and mmap write).

And then iomap applies to DAX, direct I/O, writeback, buffer read,
etc.  basically for block-based filesystems, and that is why
`struct iomap_ops` is always _one important part of iomap core_
until FUSE adapted.


But if we take a look at the overall requirement, I think what we
need is to provide an easy-use high-level fs helper library to
wrap up the following stuffs:

  1. page cache management (mainly sub-page tracking) and DAX pages;

  2. IO management (buffer I/O, direct I/O, DAX access);

  3. others (like fiemap, bmap, lseek, etc.)


For example, most block filesystems could use them of all, and for
FUSE and networking filesystem, basically they only need 1 and
maybe partial 3.

The recent implementation (including ->iomap_next()), is still
a callback-based model, the main loop is inside `fs/iomap` itself,
and you have to:

  - still arrange the mapping logic into an unique callback, which
is .iomap_next() now, if the mapping logic needs more information
rather than `struct inode *`, they have to add private pointer
somewhere.

  - Take a recent example (buffered read refactoring), need
    `struct iomap_read_folio_ctx` for FUSE and maybe other future
    networking filesystems to callback into their filesystem to
    provide new I/O contexts and submit I/Os.

But actually take a simple example of iomap_read_folio, you:
could change into (just a very rough sketch):

  xxxfs_read_folio {
    struct xxxxfs_private priv;

    iomap_iter(&iter) {   /// it could be a macro
        if (iomap_iter_outofrange(&iter))
		xxxfs_map_extent(&iter, &priv);

        // to maintain the page cache <-> struct iomap_folio_state
        // and handle some type of mapping
        if (iomap_read_folio_iter(&iter) == NEED_NEW_IO_CONTEXT) {
            xxxfs_new_io (&iter, &priv);
        }
    }
    if (xxxfs_if_any_available_io(&iter, &priv))
	xxxfs_submit_io(&iter, &priv);
}

In this model, I really think `struct iomap_read_folio_ctx` and
similar doesn't need any more (another set of callback avoidness),
and both xxxfs_map_extent() and xxxfs_new_io()/ xxxfs_submit_io()
can pass in their per-fs private without need to bother
the iomap-core to add various `private` pointers.

and in the iomap_iter() loop you can omit/change/add any part if
it doesn't fulfill to the filesystem timing.

As for "a lot of duplicate code", I think with proper new designs,
the common part could be wrapped up into macros (for example,
for bdev filesystems, xxxfs_new_io() and xxxfs_submit_io() calls
can be wrapped up into macros, and only leave:

	if (iomap_iter_outofrange(&iter))
		xxxfs_map_extent(&iter, &priv);"

can be customized for each fs as the common macro helpers.

It's just my rough thought, as I said in the previous year, I
still don't have a strong tendency but it's my suggestion over
these years.

Anyway since you're actively working/changing on iomap, so
it's fine to just respect your ideas/efforts if you don't
think it's worth, but as a record of my thoughts here again.

Thanks,
Gao Xiang

Neutral LLM

This patch series fixes reclaim storms in defrag_mode by making non-movable requests participate in pageblock production in the allocator slowpath. The changes involve small adjustments to the allocator and compaction code, including three prep patches and a fix. The series has been tested against an affected workload for two weeks and restored the OOM kill rate to baseline.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants

Zi Yan ↳ replying to Johannes Weiner Fixes Neutral LLM
Corrected the commit hash for the fixup patch. The reviewer pointed out an error in the commit message, specifically that the referenced commit was not found in the tree.
Show original comment 
On Fri Jun 26, 2026 at 2:21 PM EDT, Johannes Weiner wrote:
> As we deployed defrag_mode into Meta production, pressure spikes and
> excessive swapping were observed on some workloads. Tracing confirmed
> that this is unmovable/reclaimable requests spinning in the allocator
> and direct reclaim, causing excessive amounts of swap.
>
> The initial plan for defrag_mode was to rely on kswapd/kcompactd to
> produce blocks, and if those are overwhelmed under high pressure, let
> the allocator fall back (__rmqueue_steal()) after its retry loops.
> However, that retrying results in more reclaim on some of these
> workloads than we'd hoped, sometimes excessively so, spurred on by the
> !costly order conditions in should_reclaim_retry().
>
> The storms are dependent on the request type. Reclaim will inevitably
> make room in existing movable blocks, since that's where the LRU pages
> live. So if movable requests retry on reclaim, they make progress.
>
> When non-movable requests spin in reclaim that isn't productive. They
> cannot use the individually freed pages, and the process is unlikely
> to accidentally free whole blocks to meet the ALLOC_NOFRAGMENT bar.
> They spin and overreclaim excessively, which tanks performance and
> triggers userspace guards like swap exhaustion or pressure based OOM.
>
> To fix this, send non-movable requests, regardless of order, into
> pageblock reclaim/compaction. This way, they help move things along to
> meet the ALLOC_NOFRAGMENT bar. After this patch, the reclaim storms
> and excess OOM rates are no longer observed in production.
>
> The longer-term plan is still to have all requests, including the
> movable ones, help make blocks to spread the cost of defragmenting
> more evenly and fairly; combined with proper watermarking to reduce
> allocation latencies in the common case. However, doing this naively
> unearths scaling and concurrency limitations in compaction that need
> to be addressed first. Promoting just non-movables for now is the
> minimally viable bug fix for the above issue.
>
> Fixes: f38356df6474 ("mm: page_alloc: introduce defrag_mode")

Should be
Fixes: e3aa7df331bc ("mm: page_alloc: defrag_mode").
Since I cannot find f38356df6474 in the tree.

> Signed-off-by: Johannes Weiner <hannes@cmpxchg.org>

-- 
Best Regards,
Yan, Zi

Re: [PATCH] mm/page_alloc: bypass watermark when PCP has pages for allocation Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Last comment 2026-06-26 Re: [RFC PATCH] mm: Avoiding split large folios if swap has no space Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the Linux kernel's memory cgroup charging mechanism to avoid splitting large folios when there is no available swap space. The current implementation tries to charge against the first ancestor that fails, but this can lead to unnecessary splits. Instead, the patch proposes calculating the actual headroom by walking up the hierarchy and taking the minimum of all ancestors' available swap space. This approach aims to relax the strict limit enforcement in favor of a more flexible margin-based calculation.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants

Reviewer questioned the necessity of the patch, suggesting that a fuzz factor around the limit is acceptable and noting that the remaining headroom is less than a large folio. Reviewer Barry Song noted that the current implementation may incur significant latency for I/O or compression, and suggested splitting huge folios into 2MB folios instead of base pages when swapping out to allow architectures with large folios to use THP_SWAP. Reviewer pointed out that the patch's approach is similar to what mem_cgroup_get_nr_swap_pages() already does, suggesting that the effort may not be worth it given the limited headroom. Reviewer Barry Song suggested modifying the __mem_cgroup_try_charge_swap() function to return an additional argument for the remaining swap space, which would be used in conjunction with mem_cgroup_margin() math to determine headroom. He proposed adding a fuzz factor around the limit and provided a code concept that includes this modification.

On Thu, Jun 25, 2026 at 9:46 PM David Hildenbrand (Arm)
<david@kernel.org> wrote:

---

Yep. But we haven't enabled thp_swp for 64KB base pages yet; there
is an ongoing attempt[1].

The current concern is that such large folios may incur significant
latency for I/O or compression, and reclaim could end up waiting
for a long time.
For huge folios, maybe we should split them into 2MB folios
instead of splitting all the way down to base pages when swapping
out.
That way, we could allow architectures with "huge" large folios to swap
out using THP_SWAP?

BTW, PowerPC is enabling THP_SWPOUT for up to 16MB THP[2].

[1] https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/20251226063759.4020782-2-tongweilin@linux.alibaba.com/
[2] https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/cover.1781843449.git.ritesh.list@gmail.com/

---

It seems it is exactly what mem_cgroup_get_nr_swap_pages() is doing:

long mem_cgroup_get_nr_swap_pages(struct mem_cgroup *memcg)
{
        long nr_swap_pages = get_nr_swap_pages();

        if (mem_cgroup_disabled() || do_memsw_account())
                return nr_swap_pages;
        for (; !mem_cgroup_is_root(memcg); memcg = parent_mem_cgroup(memcg))
                nr_swap_pages = min_t(long, nr_swap_pages,
                                      READ_ONCE(memcg->swap.max) -
                                      page_counter_read(&memcg->swap));
        return nr_swap_pages;
}

---

Thanks very much to Johannes and David for the help.

the code below (just as a concept):

Last comment 2026-06-26 Re: [PATCH v3] mm: mglru: fix stale batch updates after memcg reparenting Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch fixes stale batch updates in the mm/mglru code after a memory cgroup (memcg) is reparented. The issue arises when a memcg is being destroyed and its children are being moved to another parent, causing stale LRU updates. The fix involves checking if the current memcg is being destroyed before updating its LRU batch.

Patches Submitted (2)
[PATCH v4 0/5] mm/memcontrol, page_counter: move stock from mem_cgroup to page_counter 5 patches
Show 5 individual patches
  • [1/5] mm/page_counter: introduce per-page_counter stock
  • [2/5] mm/memcontrol: flatten try_charge_memcg control flow
  • [3/5] mm/page_counter: introduce page_counter_try_charge_stock()
  • [4/5] mm/memcontrol: convert memcg to use page_counter_stock
  • [5/5] mm/memcontrol: remove unused memcg_stock code
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series moves the per-cpu stock from struct mem_cgroup to page_counter, reducing memory footprint and simplifying code. The current implementation has limitations such as a 7-memcg-per-cpu slot limit, random cross-memcg stock drains, and tight coupling with struct mem_cgroup. By moving the stock to page_counter, these issues are addressed, allowing for independent stock management and enabling additional features like tiered memory limits. However, this change introduces a potential increase in overcharged memory on large machines with many cgroups.

Last comment 2026-06-24 [PATCH] mm/memcontrol: remove unused for_each_mem_cgroup macro and cleanup Neutral LLM

This patch removes the unused `for_each_mem_cgroup` macro and cleans up related code in the Linux kernel's memory control subsystem. The macro was removed by a previous commit, but its declaration remained in the header file. This patch also updates an outdated comment and removes duplicate declarations.

Last comment 2026-06-24 Re: [PATCH v4 4/5] mm/memcontrol: convert memcg to use page_counter_stock Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch converts memcg to use page_counter_stock instead of the original hierarchical page_counter walk for uncharging. The change is motivated by concerns about stock growing without bound when using a hierarchical walk, and aims to maintain consistency with the original memcontrol code.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants

already-charged credit to the pending allocation; if the later memory charge
fails, page_counter_uncharge() discards that consumed credit from the
hierarchy. That should keeps usage equal to real charges plus remaining stock?

Re: [PATCH v4 4/5] mm/memcontrol: convert memcg to use page_counter_stock Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies
Last comment 2026-06-22 Re: [PATCH v9 3/6] mm: memcontrol: add interface for swap tier selection Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds an interface for selecting a swap tier, which is a way to control memory allocation between different types of storage. The current implementation makes tiered memcg limits a cgroup mount option, but does not allow individual memcgs to tune their limits or opt-in/out. The author suggests that making these values tunable could be considered in the future, potentially aligning with existing file names and interaction methods for memory allocation.

4 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Yosry Ahmed ↳ replying to Joshua Hahn Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Yosry Ahmed questioned whether fairness should be enforced in swap tiers, suggesting it might be necessary to prevent workloads from hogging valuable resources like faster swap tiers. Reviewer Yosry Ahmed questioned the difference between swap tiering and memory tiering, noting that while both provide a hierarchy of performance, only memory tiering allows for movement between tiers, and suggested that swap tiering may not require similar semantics.
Show original comment 
I am not sure what use cases exist, but I think it's possible we end
up wanting to enforce fairness for swap tiers as well. Maybe not as
aggressively as memory (e.g. to avoid wearing out SSDs), but maybe at
least proactively through userspace?

At the end of the day, faster swap tiers are also valuable resources
that we probably don't want a few workloads to hog. I also think the
interfaces being consistent makes everyone's lives easier, even if
it's a bit of an overkill for swap tiers.

---

Hmm I don't follow. It's also possible (maybe not initially) that a
memcg cannot use specific memory tiers, right? I am not sure what the
difference is.

Joshua Hahn (author) ↳ replying to Yosry Ahmed Needs Work LLM

Show original comment 
No, you're right about that. Sorry for steering the thread to my 
series ; -)

To get back to the question of how the auto-tuning should work, the
main question is to which ratio we scale the swap limits to.
Do we set the swap limits proportional to how much swap is present
in the system, or how much swap is available to the cgroup?

So if we have 3 swap tiers A, B, C, with 50G, 30G, and 20G capacity

Last comment 2026-06-23 Re: [PATCH v9 3/6] mm: memcontrol: add interface for swap tier selection Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds an interface for swap tier selection in the Linux kernel's memory control system, addressing concerns about accidental overcommitting of resources when using multiple workloads and tiers. The approach taken is to provide a default 'reasonable' scaling that users can override if needed, rather than allowing opt-in/opt-out for individual tiers. This change aims to simplify the configuration process while preventing potential resource overcommit issues.

4 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Yosry Ahmed ↳ replying to Joshua Hahn Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer questioned whether disabling/enabling a swap tier should change the limits of other tiers, noting that recalculating all limits could lead to inconsistent behavior if custom limits are allowed.
Show original comment 
That's a good question. I think the case that is particularly
interesting is whether or not the limits of other tiers should change
when another tier is disabled/enabled.

So basically in your example, assuming everything starts as "max",
when swap.max is set to 10G, the autoscaled limits would be: (tier A,
5G), (tier B, 3G), (tier C, 2G). Now the question becomes, if
userspace sets the limit of tier C to 0, should the limits for tiers A
and B change?

On one hand, it's simpler to just keep the autoscaled limits unchanged
in this case. However, this means that the effective swap limit is now
8G, which is not great :/

The alternative is to recalculate all the limits when one of them
changes, in which case the limits of A and B would change to 6.25G and
3.75G. But I don't know if this will work well if we allow custom
limits. What happens if the limit of tier C is written as 1 (or 4096)
instead of 0? It's effectively the same scenario, but the tier is
technically allowed.

The more I think about it, the more I realize it may be best to drop
the autoscaling thing. I imagine memory tiering might run into similar
issues too :/
View on lore ↗
Signals: requested changes, alternative solution
Joshua Hahn (author) ↳ replying to Yosry Ahmed Neutral LLM
Author acknowledges a potential issue with swap tier selection, specifically the risk of overcommitting when excluding certain tiers from workloads, and expresses uncertainty about how concerning this is. Author addressed Yosry Ahmed's concern about how to scale swap limits for cgroups, explaining that if system-wide ratios are used, there's no need to change the ratios at all and users can always override per-tier limits.
Show original comment 
I think the one problem with this is that it becomes quite easy to
accidentally overcommit. As a toy example, if you have 10 workloads and
100G swap (as in the example I gave above), intuitively setting
swap.max = 10G for all 10 workloads shouldn't ever cause any contention
on capacity. But if you start excluding some tiers from some workloads,
you actually get overcommitting on the tiers that can service the
most workloads.

I am not sure how concerning swap overcommit was, but at least in the
memory tiering scenario accidental overcommitting of toptier memory
seemed bad enough that I wanted to avoid the problem entirely.

---

And that's why I didn't include opt-in/opt-out for any of the tiers;
if you have system-wide ratios, there's no need to change the ratios
at all, and as long as the sum of your memory.limit for each workload
is under the total capacity, all tiers will also not be overcommitted.

Now, all of these complications aside, I think we might be overthinking
a bit here : -) The auto-scaling should just provide some sort of
"reasonable" default, the users can always override the per-tier
limits if they are unhappy with the autoscaled values.

In fact, maybe it even makes sense to have sum of swap tier limits >
swap.max.

(I actually recall having a really similar discussion when I was working
on weighted interleave auto-tuning a year ago, on how weights should be
set when switching between manually-set limits and relying on
auto-scaled defaults [1]. I don't think there's a need to follow this
convention, but we should think about what the expected behavior should
be if a user manually sets a limit, but later wants to go back to
auto-scaling limits). 

Anyways, I think these are important questions. Youngjun, Nhat, Shakeel,
any thoughts from you all? : -)

[1] https://lore.kernel.org/all/8734hbiq7j.fsf@DESKTOP-5N7EMDA/
View on lore ↗
Signals: uncertainty, acknowledgment_of_issue, overthinking

Reviewer Yosry Ahmed noted that both presenting a default value for swap tier selection and allowing users to manually set limits can lead to overcommitting issues, and suggested removing the default value so that 'max' is the default for all tiers, allowing userspace to set custom limits if needed.

some memory tiers. For example, limit sensitive workloads to the top

I agree, but it seems like both options are not ideal here. I think it
might make more sense to not present a default value at all, have

isn't. Userspace can set the limits if they need to. Autoscaling the

Joshua Hahn (author) ↳ replying to Yosry Ahmed Needs Work LLM
Author acknowledges a concern about system-critical processes being demoted in tiered memcg limit model and agrees that they should be prioritized. Author addressed Yosry Ahmed's concern about accidental overcommitting in swap tier selection by proposing to make swap tiers a no-op unless users opt-in to setting limits themselves, and suggested changing the memory tiering series to not present default settings for tiered limits.
Show original comment 
Yup, that makes sense to me too.

One of the things that did concern me a bit with my model for tiered
memcg limit was that system-critical processes would also be susceptible
to being demoted and churned, when we would much rather make sure
those are kept protected at the toptier.

---

I like this idea a lot. That would basically make swap tiers a no-op
unless you opt-into setting the limits yourself, so we don't run the
risk of accidentally enabling tiers.

On that note, maybe it makes sense for me to change my
memory tiering series to also just not present a default setting for
tiered limits, and instead just set them as max until the user comes
and configures them?

have more to say on this. Johannes, Michal, Roman, and Shakeel,
what do you guys think? Could an approach to just make the memory
tier limits writable from the get-go and not expose any defaults
make sense to you?

I think that would simplify the code quite a bit and also help mitigate
the possible side effects on system-critical workloads.

Thanks!
Joshua

Last comment 2026-06-26 [PATCH] Documentation/bpf: make it clear that kfuncs should be non-static Neutral LLM

The patch updates the kfuncs documentation to clarify that kernel functions (kfuncs) should not be declared static. This is because static linkage can cause issues with BTF resolution and function renaming, which can break the externally visible name required for kfunc resolution.

2 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants

Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-17 Last comment 2026-06-19 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH] mm/bpf_memcontrol: mark BPF memcg kfuncs static Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch marks several BPF memory control functions in bpf_memcontrol.c as static, since they are only referenced by BPF programs and resolved through BTF (Binary Interface Format). This change is made to remove unnecessary external linkage. The affected functions include bpf_get_root_mem_cgroup, bpf_get_mem_cgroup, bpf_put_mem_cgroup, bpf_mem_cgroup_vm_events, and bpf_mem_cgroup_usage. These changes are based on the introduction of BPF kfuncs in previous commits (5904db9891f8, 5c7db3239c9f, and 99430ab8b804). The patch is reported to fix a kernel test robot issue and closes a related bug report.

{
      "type": "Dead Code / Linker Error",

      "description": "Marking kfuncs as static could cause the compiler
      to optimize them away, breaking BPF verifier BTF resolution.",

      "reasoning": "The patch adds `static` to multiple `__bpf_kfunc`
      annotated functions that are not called anywhere in C
      code. Normally, a compiler would remove unused static functions or
      fail to expose them in object files, which could prevent the BTF
      ID generation logic (`resolve_btfids`) from finding them. However,
      the `__bpf_kfunc` macro expands to include `__used` and
      `__retain`, which strictly instructs the compiler and linker to
      preserve the functions in the object file regardless of
      usage. Additionally, `resolve_btfids` and `BTF_ID_FLAGS`
      successfully parse and resolve `STB_LOCAL` static symbols, making
      this change completely safe and correct.",

          functions modified by the patch."

Alexei Starovoitov ↳ replying to Roman Gushchin Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
The reviewer, Alexei Starovoitov, pointed out that using 'static' instead of '__used' or '__retain' may lead to function renaming by the compiler in LTO mode, and suggested updating the documentation to address this issue.
Show original comment 
Almost, except it's not clear what takes priority for the compiler.
__bpf_kfunc used to have '__used' and it was enough until clang
got too aggressive in LTO mode. So we added '__retain' and so far
it seems to be holding, but sashiko is missing that both of these
attributes don't say anything about _name_ of the function.
'static' keyword means that compiler is free to rename it and
compilers do take advantage of that from time to time,
while __used and __retain don't add 'do-not-rename' restriction
to the compiler.

Also look at it from the other angle.. why anyone would add
'static' when the function is clearly not static to this particular
compilation unit. It will be used outside of it by bpf progs
that are obviuosly not part of this .c file.

Documentation/bpf/kfuncs.rst needs to be updated.

JP,
pls send a patch to update the docs and include few words
to ignore sparse.

[PATCH v5] mm/lruvec: trace LRU add drains and drain-all requests Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds two new tracepoints to the Linux kernel's memory management (mm) subsystem. The first, mm_lru_add_drain, reports when an LRU add batch is drained and includes information about the CPU and number of folios in the batch. This allows tracing to distinguish between full drains and partial drains. The second, mm_lru_add_drain_all, captures callers of __lru_add_drain_all and whether they set the force flag for all CPUs. These tracepoints are implemented as bare trace hooks using DECLARE_TRACE(), which avoids creating a new trace event ABI. This patch addresses LRU lock contention issues by providing more detailed tracing information.

Last comment 2026-06-23 [PATCH v3] mm/page_alloc: use existing highatomic reserves on the buddy fastpath Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the Linux kernel's page allocation mechanism to allow high-priority non-blocking allocations to use existing MIGRATE_HIGHATOMIC reserves on the buddy fastpath without growing them. The patch tightens the criteria for reserving pageblocks so that growth only occurs in the slowpath, and introduces a new function gfp_to_alloc_flags_nonblocking() to enable this behavior. This change improves performance by reducing the number of highatomic misses observed during a UDP receive workload.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-17 Last comment 2026-06-22 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v4 0/4] arm64: cross-CPU NMI via SDEI Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series adds a new mechanism to deliver NMI (Non-Maskable Interrupt) across CPUs on arm64 architecture using SDEI (System Description Event Infrastructure). This is necessary for certain debug and observability features, such as capturing CPU backtraces and crash dumps, which require interrupting a CPU that has its interrupts locally masked. The existing pseudo-NMI mechanism is too expensive in terms of performance overhead, so this new approach uses SDEI to deliver the NMI at a lower cost. This allows for more accurate debugging and observability features without introducing significant performance regressions.

Kiryl Shutsemau (author) ↳ replying to Marc Zyngier Neutral LLM
The author is addressing concerns about the performance cost of pseudo-NMI on arm64 systems. They acknowledge that previous numbers were unreliable and provide fresh measurements from NVIDIA Grace (Neoverse V2) showing a measurable cost, but claim it's a constant per-syscall tax rather than a contention effect. Author acknowledges that FEAT_NMI is the long-term solution, but expresses concern about a decade-long gap where existing silicon will not have either FEAT_NMI or pseudo-NMI, and argues that their SDEI-based patch is a minimal use of a deprecated interface to cover this gap. Author asks for an alternative solution if the current approach is unacceptable.
Show original comment 
Full disclosure: the ~66% figures come from internal testing about a year ago.
I no longer have the details of the machine it ran on and can't confirm whether
ICC_CTLR_EL1.PMHE was set there -- it may well have been. I shouldn't have
carried those numbers forward without being able to stand behind them, so
please disregard them.

Here are fresh numbers from NVIDIA Grace (Neoverse V2). Importantly, this
box reports:

  GICv3: Pseudo-NMIs enabled using relaxed ICC_PMR_EL1 synchronisation

i.e. PMHE == 0, so the synchronising DSB on the unmask path is already
patched to a NOP (ARM64_HAS_GIC_PRIO_RELAXED_SYNC). What's left is the
floor cost of PMR-based masking itself plus the PMR save/restore on
exception entry/exit -- not the DSB. So this is the case Catalin asked
about (DSB omitted), and there is still a measurable cost.

A trivial single-threaded gettid() loop (1e6 calls, median of 5,
performance governor, ASLR off):

  pseudo_nmi=0 (DAIF):       178.4 ns/call
  pseudo_nmi=1 (PMR):        252.5 ns/call
  delta:                     +74.1 ns/call  (~230-250 cycles)
                             +41.5% wall time / 0.706 throughput

  --- u-bench.c ---
  #include <unistd.h>
  #include <sys/syscall.h>
  #include <time.h>
  #include <stdio.h>
  int main(void) {
          struct timespec a, b;
          clock_gettime(CLOCK_MONOTONIC, &a);
          for (long i = 0; i < 1000000; i++)
                  syscall(SYS_gettid);
          clock_gettime(CLOCK_MONOTONIC, &b);
          printf("%f ns\n", (b.tv_sec-a.tv_sec)*1e9 + (b.tv_nsec-a.tv_nsec));
          return 0;
  }

will-it-scale agrees independently. sched_yield (ops/s, median of 5):

                      1 task       72 tasks
  pseudo_nmi=0      3,195,656    230,824,534
  pseudo_nmi=1      2,253,753    163,914,837
  ratio                0.705          0.710

The ratio is flat across the whole 1-to-72 sweep, so -- relevant to the
scalability question -- it's a constant per-syscall tax, not a contention
effect. The impact tracks syscall/exception density: page_fault1, a more
realistic workload, stays within ~5%.

---

I understand FEAT_NMI is the long-term answer, and I'm not arguing against
deprecating SDEI. My concern is the gap in between. By our estimate it's
10+ years before the last non-FEAT_NMI machine retires from the fleet --
for scale, we're still running Skylake today. So there's roughly a
decade where a large installed base has neither FEAT_NMI nor affordable
pseudo-NMI, and no way to reach a DAIF-masked CPU for an all-CPU
backtrace or to capture a wedged CPU in a crash dump. That's the
functional gap this series tries to cover.

Given the deprecation direction, I deliberately kept the SDEI footprint as
small as I could. The series adds no new firmware interface and no vendor
SMC -- it uses only the standard software-signalled event (event 0) via
SDEI_EVENT_SIGNAL, which is already present on these systems for
firmware-first RAS (APEI/GHES). And SDEI is only ever invoked in a "bad
state": to deliver a backtrace signal to a CPU that a normal IPI can't
reach, or to stop a CPU that ignored the stop IPIs. Nothing on any hot or
steady-state path touches it.

If even that minimal use is unacceptable on a deprecated interface, I'd
rather know now and redirect the effort -- but I'd appreciate a pointer to
what should cover this gap for existing silicon in the meantime.

-- 
  Kiryl Shutsemau / Kirill A. Shutemov

Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-17 Last comment 2026-06-17 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v4 1/4] firmware: arm_sdei: add sdei_is_present() Neutral LLM

The patch introduces a new function `sdei_is_present()` that checks whether SDEI firmware is present and usable on the system. This allows optional consumers to skip registering events and avoid warnings when there is no SDEI firmware.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-17 ⏱ No comments yet [PATCH v4 2/4] firmware: arm_sdei: add SDEI_EVENT_SIGNAL support Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch adds a new function called sdei_event_signal() to the arm_sdei firmware driver, which signals a software-signalled event (event 0) to another processor element (PE). The function is designed to be safe to call from non-maskable interrupt (NMI) or crash context and takes no locks. It uses the SDEI_EVENT_SIGNAL call to deliver the event NMI-like even when interrupts are masked on the target PE. The patch also updates the include files to reflect the new function.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-17 Last comment 2026-06-18 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v4 3/4] drivers/firmware: add SDEI cross-CPU NMI service for arm64 Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds support for delivering NMI-like events to interrupt-masked arm64 CPUs using the SDEI software-signalled event mechanism. The goal is to provide a more efficient way to deliver such events, avoiding the pseudo-NMI hot-path cost. The first user of this feature is arch_trigger_cpumask_backtrace(), which can now reach masked CPUs without falling back to an IPI path when SDEI is available. A stub implementation is provided for cases where SDEI is not present or disabled.

Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-17 Last comment 2026-06-17 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH v4 4/4] arm64: escalate smp_send_stop() to an SDEI NMI as a last resort Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch adds a new mechanism to the Linux kernel on arm64 architecture to handle CPU wedging by escalating smp_send_stop() to an SDEI NMI (Non-Maskable Interrupt) as a last resort. When a CPU is wedged with interrupts masked, it ignores stop IPIs and without pseudo-NMI there's no NMI IPI to escalate to. The patch introduces a third rung in smp_send_stop() that signals an SDEI event 0 at the remaining online CPUs, which firmware delivers regardless of the target's DAIF (Debug Access Interface Flag). This allows the CPU to reach a wedged CPU and acknowledge by going offline. The stop bookkeeping is also folded into a shared function arm64_nmi_cpu_stop() for consistency.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants

Reviewer Doug Anderson expressed concern that the SDEI NMI handler may not be able to handle CPUs that are in a wedged state, as it relies on firmware delivering the event regardless of the target's DAIF (Device Access Interrupt Flag), but this assumption may not hold for all firmware implementations. Last comment 2026-06-24 Re: mm/hwpoison: persist poisoned PFN list across kexec via KHO [RFC] Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch addresses a problem where poisoned PFN (Page Frame Number) lists are not persisted across kexec, which can lead to memory failures in the new kernel. The issue arises because some memory allocations occur before buddy is initialized and can land on bad frames. To solve this, the patch proposes using EFI_UNUSABLE_MEMORY to mark non-usable memory as nomap, making it invisible to both allocators and KASLR. This approach ensures that the bad frame is not accessible in the new kernel.

3 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Pratyush Yadav Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Pratyush Yadav suggested denying kexec if any pages in the scratch memory area are poisoned, as this would prevent unreliable booting; he also noted that an early check for poisoned pages is necessary due to recent changes
Show original comment 
With KHO, you have "scratch memory", a pre-reserved area of memory on
cold boot. The kernel image is always in this area when KHO is used. I
think it would be a fair idea to deny kexec if any of the pages in this
scratch area are poisoned. Because at that point you can't reliably boot
anyway.

Normally, all allocations between setup_arch() and memblock_free_all()
_also_ happen from scratch memory, so this check would solve the first
problem too... but I recently added patches [0] to change this. So I
think we do need to identify the poisoned pages early in boot.

[0] https://lore.kernel.org/kexec/20260605183501.3884950-16-pratyush@kernel.org/

[...]

-- 
Regards,
Pratyush Yadav

Last comment 2026-06-25 [PATCH] btrfs: replace writeback inhibition xarray with a fixed inline buffer Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch replaces the xarray used to track inhibited extent buffers in Btrfs transactions with a fixed inline buffer, managed by a CLOCK eviction policy. This change addresses two issues: a write_iops regression caused by frequent xarray stores during COW operations and an unbounded cleanup walk due to long iteration over inhibited buffers. The new approach uses a small fixed-size array to track the working set of buffers revisited across search_again descents, with a CLOCK eviction policy that ensures frequently accessed buffers are retained while less frequently accessed ones are reclaimed first.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Sun YangKai ↳ replying to Leo Martins Inline Review Reviewed-by Neutral LLM
Reviewer Sun YangKai noted that the design is clean but suggested non-blocking improvements Reviewer Sun YangKai suggested using an overflowing add operation with masking to replace the modulo operator when indexing into the fixed inline buffer, and questioned the usefulness of storing additional information in the higher bits. The reviewer suggested extracting the inhibit preparation logic into a helper function, likely to improve code organization and readability. Reviewer Sun YangKai noted that using u8 for inhibited_ebs fields is a straightforward choice, even if the limit is later extended to 16 slots, and only the bitmap field would need to be widened to u16.
Show original comment 
Reviewed-by: Sun YangKai <sunk67188@gmail.com>

Thanks for your great work. The overall direction looks nice and the 
design is clean. I've got some non\u2011blocking suggestions, though.

---

I think we can using overflowing add with ` & (SLOTS - 1)` as the index 
as long as SLOTS is power of 2, instead of `% SLOTS`. And the higher 
bits can parsed as number of "rounds" if we keep them, and I'm not sure 
if the extra info is useful for any purpose.

---

And personally I'd love to wrap this part of logic into a helper 
function like

static inline u8 btrfs_inhibit_prepare_slot(struct btrfs_trans_handle 
*trans) {...}

Or something with a better name.

---

Given the 8-slot limit for inhibited_ebs (4 bits (8) for capacity, 8 
bits for bitmap), using u8 for these two fields is a clear and 
straightforward choice IMO. Even if we later extend the limit to 16, 
only the bitmap field (inhibited_ebs_referenced) would need to be 
widened to u16.

Thanks,
Sun YangKai
