  • [3/7] mm, swap: support physical swap as a vswap backend Neutral Submitted 2026-06-12Last comment 2026-06-23 Yosry Ahmed Nhat Pham (author)
  • [4/7] mm, swap: only charge physical swap entries Submitted 2026-06-12
  • [5/7] mm, swap: add debugfs counters for vswap Submitted 2026-06-12
  • [6/7] mm, swap: defer memcg_table allocation on physical clusters Submitted 2026-06-12
  • [7/7] mm, swap: widen swap_info_struct max/pages to unsigned long Submitted 2026-06-12
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series introduces Virtual Swap Space (Swap Table Edition), which aims to address the limitations of current swap design by decoupling swap entries from physical backing slots. This allows for more efficient use of disk space, especially in scenarios where zswap is used. The changes include rebasing to a newer mm-unstable tip, fixing assorted issues, and simplifying code. Additionally, the patch series introduces new features such as deferring memcg_table allocation on physical clusters until the first vswap-backing slot installs and widening swap_info_struct->max and ->pages to support up to 8 PB of swap space.

[PATCH 0/3] convert iova to maple tree 3 patches
Show 3 individual patches
  • [1/3] iova: convert from rbtree to maple tree
  • [2/3] iova: add KUnit test suite
  • [3/3] iova: defer maple tree erase on GFP_ATOMIC failure
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch series converts the iova (Input/Output Virtual Address) allocation code to use a maple tree data structure, which improves performance and reduces the likelihood of soft lockups caused by linear search in alloc_iova(). The patches also add self-tests for the iova code and reduce memory usage. The changes were tested on both virtual machines and physical systems with IOMMU enabled.

Last comment 2026-06-25 [PATCH v2 0/3] mm: __access_remote_vm with per-VMA lock 3 patches
Show 3 individual patches
  • [1/3] x86/mm: use READ_ONCE/WRITE_ONCE for mm->context.untag_mask
  • [2/3] mm/pagewalk: let folio_walk_start() run under the per-VMA lock Neutral Last comment 2026-06-25 Lorenzo Stoakes Rik Riel (author)
  • [3/3] mm: read remote memory without the mmap lock where possible Neutral Last comment 2026-06-25 Lorenzo Stoakes
Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch series introduces a fast path for __access_remote_vm that uses per-VMA locks instead of the process-wide mmap_lock, improving system monitoring reliability and speeding up the function under contention. The fast path is only enabled when it's safe to do so, requiring a kernel with per-VMA locks, RCU page table freeing, and specific VMA types excluded. This change aims to address issues where processes get stuck holding the mmap_lock for extended periods, hindering system monitoring tools' ability to assess and log situations.

Rik Riel (author) ↳ replying to Lorenzo Stoakes Neutral LLM
The author is addressing a concern about the reliability of system monitoring under mmap_lock contention, specifically during THP collapse. They acknowledge that relying only on the RCU read lock to read the page table could result in seeing old page table contents that no longer point at the current process memory. The author suggests two possible ways for khugepaged to fix this issue and notes that it currently uses the first approach.
Show original comment 
One issue here is that while we can safely read
the old page table under the RCU read lock, in
the middle of a THP collapse there is no guarantee
that the old page table points at the process's 
current memory.

Khugepaged could fix this in one of two ways:
- zap all readers with an IPI, and use that as
  synchronization
- make sure the old page table's PTEs point at
  the individual pages inside the new PMD

Right now khugepaged does the first.

Relying only on the RCU read lock to read the
page table could result in us seeing old page
table contents, that no longer point at the
current process memory.

Unless I'm missing something...

--

Shakeel Butt

11 items
Patches Submitted (1)
Last comment 2026-06-26 [PATCH] mm/slub: serve slabobj_ext array from a strictly larger kmalloc cache Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch fixes a kernel stack overflow issue caused by a recursive cycle between kmalloc-512 and kmalloc-1k slabs when freeing resources. The problem occurs because the obj_exts array, used for allocation profiling and memcg accounting, is allocated from the same cache as the slab it describes. To fix this, the patch changes the allocation to use a strictly larger cache than the one described by the slab, breaking the cycle and preventing the stack overflow.

1 reviewer comment — 0 participants
0 participants
Harry Yoo ↳ replying to Shakeel Butt Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Harry Yoo noted that the patch description does not clearly explain why the current solution in commit 280ea9c3154b is insufficient to prevent the stack overflow, and requested a more detailed explanation of the fix. The reviewer noted that the patch's assumption about KMALLOC_NORMAL caches not needing obj_exts arrays may be incorrect and suggested considering a scenario where memory allocation profiling is enabled. Reviewer Harry Yoo noted that the proposed fix will waste memory due to a larger obj_exts array size, and suggested creating a new kmalloc type to avoid cycles instead. Reviewer Harry Yoo pointed out that the patch's logic is no longer relevant due to a change in how object sizes are compared, and suggested that the comparison should be done based on cache pointers instead of object size.
Show original comment 
Hi Shakeel,

On 6/26/26 8:00 AM, Shakeel Butt wrote:

Usually KMALLOC_NORMAL caches don't need obj_exts array, but yes,
this could happen if memory allocation profiling is enabled.

---

This will fix the problem.

But this will waste memory as we need smaller obj_exts array
as the size gets larger.

We should probably create a new kmalloc type to avoid cycles instead?
(needed only when memory profiling is enabled, though)

That would also prevent recursion even further.

Reviewer Harry Yoo noted that the patch's mem_alloc_profiling_enabled() check is unnecessary because it doesn't address the issue on SLUB_TINY, and suggested removing it to avoid pessimizing memory allocation profiling and SLUB_TINY && MEMCG users. He also proposed adding a comment to explain why obj_exts allocations cannot form a cycle between kmalloc caches when allocated from non-KMALLOC_NORMAL caches.
Show original comment 
We should not add mem_alloc_profiling_enabled() check because,
then we're not fixing this issue on SLUB_TINY, when the caller specifies
__GFP_RECLAIMABLE|__GFP_ACCOUNT without memory allocation profiling.

`if (!is_kmalloc_normal(s))` check already bails out when it doesn't
need to bump the size.

So Shakeel's original code will work fine.

We're only pessimizing memory allocation profiling and
SLUB_TINY && MEMCG users, but (as Vlastimil suggests off-list)
it wouldn't make much sense to enable MEMCG on memory restricted systems
anyway. (IIRC even raspberry pis don't enable the memory controller by
default...)

I think it's okay to fix the bug first, but we need to address
the memory wastage issue sooner or later if companies (Meta and
Google I guess?) are deploying kernels with memory allocation profiling
on in production systems.

Perhaps it's worth adding a comment like this, though:

/*
 * Only bump the size when the object (not the obj_exts array) is
 * allocated from KMALLOC_NORMAL, either by memory allocation profiling
 * or memcg on SLUB_TINY with __GFP_RECLAIMABLE|__GFP_ACCOUNT.
 * Otherwise, obj_exts allocations cannot form a cycle between
 * kmalloc caches.
 */
if (!is_kmalloc_normal(s))
        return sz;

Thanks!

-- 
Cheers,
Harry / Hyeonggon

Harry Yoo ↳ replying to Suren Baghdasaryan Inline Review Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
Reviewer Harry Yoo noted that the patch's logic for returning a larger kmalloc cache size is incorrect and suggested an alternative condition to check for mem_alloc_profiling_enabled() Reviewer Harry Yoo noted that the patch's use of a new KMALLOC_TYPE may not be ideal in the long term, as it is based on GFP flags and does not provide a clear abstraction for slab object size, whereas SLAB_BUCKETS infrastructure introduced in v6.11 provides a more straightforward solution. Show original comment 
[ Adding Kees Cook for SLAB_BUCKETS conversation ]

The thread:
https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/20260625230029.703750-1-shakeel.butt@linux.dev/

On 6/29/26 8:37 AM, Suren Baghdasaryan wrote:

---

As mentioned by Vlsatimil, in the long term, using SLAB_BUCKETS
infrastructure would be more straightforward than new KMALLOC_TYPE
because (I think) the kmalloc type is decided purely based on GFP
flags and we need to somehow work around that. SLAB_BUCKETS provides
a nice abstraction to do this.

Luckily, SLAB_BUCKETS is introduced in v6.11.
Unfortunately, SLAB_BUCKETS is optional.

---

I think we need some enhancements to achieve that with SLAB_BUCKETS

1. Rename SLAB_BUCKETS to SLAB_BUCKETS_HARDENING
   (w/ SLAB_BUCKETS being a transitional config for _HARDENING)

2. Make the SLAB_BUCKETS infrastructure unconditional,
   but the decision is made at runtime:

   1) actually creating a kmem_buckets vs.
   2) falling back to kmalloc.

3. kmem_buckets_create() creates kmem_buckets only when
   SLAB_BUCKETS_HARDENING is enabled.

4. SLUB decides (not) to create kmem_buckets for internal use
   during the boot process. Use the kmem_buckets for obj_exts
   array allocation.

Side note: this would unconditionally add the kmem_buckets parameter to
the kmalloc slowpath. Probably it'd be worth introducing a dedicated
entrypoint for kmem_buckets instead.

---

Since above requires quite some changes, I'd say let's proeed with
the fix (since it's one line of code change that fixes a bug),
and then see how we can make SLAB_BUCKETS changes as minimal
as possible for backporting?

-- 
Cheers,
Harry / Hyeonggon

Last comment 2026-06-27 Re: [PATCH v2 1/4] mm: avoid unnecessary lru drain for wp_can_reuse_anon_folio() Neutral LLM

Patch aims to avoid unnecessary LRU drain for wp_can_reuse_anon_folio(), but discussion centers around whether this is necessary and its potential impact on lru cache effectiveness.

Raised concerns about premature LRU drain potentially making lru lock contention worse for workloads that churn a lot of LRU pages through allocation, free, and/or reclaim.

Barry Song ↳ replying to Shakeel Butt Neutral LLM

Show original comment 
On Sat, Jun 27, 2026 at 10:44 AM Shakeel Butt <shakeel.butt@linux.dev> wrote:

> On Fri, Jun 26, 2026 at 06:25:48PM +0200, David Hildenbrand (Arm) wrote:

> > > <david@kernel.org> wrote:
> > >>
> > >> On 6/24/26 01:16, Barry Song (Xiaomi) wrote:
> > >>> We always unconditionally drain the LRU before retrying anon folio
> > >>> reuse in wp_can_reuse_anon_folio(). Instead, assume !LRU anon folios
> > >>> are in lru_cache, and use the refcount to avoid many unnecessary LRU
> > >>> drains.
> > >>>
> > >>> Acked-by: Shakeel Butt <shakeel.butt@linux.dev>
> > >>> Reviewed-by: Baoquan He <baoquan.he@linux.dev>

> > >>> --- a/mm/memory.c
> > >>> +++ b/mm/memory.c
> > >>> @@ -4193,12 +4193,18 @@ static bool wp_can_reuse_anon_folio(struct folio *folio,
> > >>>        */
> > >>>       if (folio_test_ksm(folio) || folio_ref_count(folio) > 3)
> > >>>               return false;

> > >>> +     if (!folio_test_lru(folio)) {
> > >>> +             /*
> > >>> +              * Assume folio is on lru_cache and holds a cache reference.

> > >>> +             if (folio_ref_count(folio) > 2 + folio_test_swapcache(folio))
> > >>> +                     return false;

> > >
> > > "I booted the system into Ubuntu, and after
> >
> > is this with this patch only?

Right. wp_reuse_skipped_drain accounts for the behavior
introduced by this patch, while do_swap_skipped_drain comes
from patch 3/3.

> >
> > >
> > > boot completed I observed:
> > >
> > > wp_reuse_skipped_drain: 5542
> > > do_swap_skipped_drain: 0
> > >
> > > Then I built the kernel in a 1GB memcg using zRAM swap, and observed:
> > >
> > > wp_reuse_skipped_drain: 25017
> > > do_swap_skipped_drain: 43595
> >
> > This is all data that belongs into this patch description, not hidden
> > somewhere on the internet :)

Agreed. Sorry, I was being a bit lazy.

>
> I asked the same i.e. to include this information in the commit message [1].
>
> [1] https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/ajK4N2zK3sPZFQuf@linux.dev/

Sorry, I missed this one. I'll add it in v3.

>
> >
> >
> > And why do we care about local draining? This is not a drain-all.
>
> Let me take a stab at it. Local draining can potentially make the lru cache's
> batching ineffective. The whole reason for lru caches is to batch the lru
> operations to keep the lru lock contention low but if we keep draining
> prematurely we will continue to make lru lock contention worse. This is
> particularly bad for workloads which chrun a lot of LRU pages through
> allocation, free and/or reclaim.

I actually asked the same question myself in the RFC here[1]:

"On the other hand, the folio may be sitting in the lru_cache of
another CPU, which the current drain cannot flush. As things stand
today, the drain only succeeds if the folio happens to be queued on
the current CPU's lru_cache, giving it roughly a 1/nr_cpus chance of
working. drain_all would clearly be too expensive to avoid.
So another possibility is to drop this drain as well. The folio can
be released later, at the cost of missing some opportunities for
reuse."

After thinking about it for a couple of days, my gut feeling is
that keeping it might increase the chances of reuse in at least
two cases:
1. It could be a newly allocated folio in do_swap_page(), and
do_swap_page() may subsequently call do_wp_page(). In
that case, task migration is less likely to have occurred.
2. On systems with fewer CPUs (and typically less memory), the
chance of having the folio in the same CPU's lru_cache,
which is roughly 1 / nr_cpus, may still be reasonably high.

I'm not entirely sure about this, so I chose a relatively
conservative approach: making an obviously correct improvement
without introducing dramatic changes. Another reason is that it's
also hard to evaluate the impact of removing the local drain
across all systems and workloads.

Shakeel, David, what are your thoughts on this? Should we go
with a code refinement in v3 as David suggested, or completely
remove this drain?

[1] https://lore.kernel.org/linux-mm/CAGsJ_4yFgcvpTAZ_YV73+U=BEnOiSxaFtGNFpbf0DWBkVcOQ3Q@mail.gmail.com/

Best Regards
Barry

Show original comment 
Hi Shakeel,

On 6/26/26 4:22 AM, Shakeel Butt wrote:
> On Thu, Jun 25, 2026 at 11:15:54PM +0800, Qi Zheng wrote:
>> From: Qi Zheng <zhengqi.arch@bytedance.com>
>>
>> The mglru page table walker batches per-generation size deltas in
>> walk->nr_pages while walking page tables without holding the lruvec lock.
>> The reset_batch_size() later folds those deltas into walk->lruvec under
>> the lruvec lock.
>>
>> The page table walker can run concurrently with the memcg reparenting path
>> as follows:
>>
>> CPU0                           CPU1
>> ====                           ====
>>
>> walk_mm
>> --> walk_page_range
>>      --> update_batch_size
>>          --> walk->nr_pages += delta
>>
>>                                mem_cgroup_css_offline
>>                                --> memcg_reparent_objcgs
>>                                    --> lock lruvec
>>                                        lru_gen_reparent_memcg
>>                                        --> reparent child folios to parent
>>                                        unlock lruvec
>>
>>      lock lruvec
>>      reset_batch_size
>>      --> child lrugen->nr_pages += delta
>>
>> This will trigger the following warning in lru_gen_exit_memcg():
>>
>> 	VM_WARN_ON_ONCE(memchr_inv(lruvec->lrugen.nr_pages, 0,
>> 				   sizeof(lruvec->lrugen.nr_pages)));
>>
>> And the user-visible impact of underestimated nr_pages in MGLRU was
>> premature OOMs because MGLRU does not try to reclaim memory when nr_pages
>> reaches zero, but there are still more pages.
>>
>> To fix it, make reset_batch_size() check CSS_DYING under RCU before
>> flushing the pending batch. A non-dying memcg keeps the original lruvec
>> stable against RCU-delayed offlining; a dying memcg redirects the deltas
>> to the first non-dying ancestor.
>>
>> Reported-by: Peiyang He <peiyang_he@smail.nju.edu.cn>
>> Closes: https://lore.kernel.org/all/5A9E929D82717101+12fcf643-efb8-4b9a-a53a-1e28cc894f0b@smail.nju.edu.cn
>> Fixes: f304652609ea ("mm: vmscan: prepare for reparenting MGLRU folios")

Patches Submitted (3)
Ongoing Submitted 2026-06-09 Last comment 2026-06-23 ⏱ No recent activity [PATCH] fs/super: skip non-memcg-aware nr_cached_objects in memcg slab shrink Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch modifies the Linux kernel's super_block shrinker to skip non-memcg-aware nr_cached_objects hooks during per-memcg slab reclaim. The issue arises when memcg-heavy slab reclaim is performed, causing hundreds of duplicate work items to be queued and resulting in wasted CPU cycles. To address this, the patch restricts the invocation of these global fs-specific hooks to only occur on the root/global shrink path, ensuring that the actual reclaim happens on the global path rather than per-memcg.

4 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants

[RFC 0/1] mm/vmscan: reduce lru_lock contention via vmstat-derived scan-balance cost
Show 1 individual patches
  • [1/1] mm/vmscan: reduce lru_lock contention via vmstat-derived scan-balance cost
Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch reduces contention on the lru_lock by moving the cost-recording work to a dedicated per-lruvec lock, rather than having multiple producers update the struct lruvec under lru_lock. The anon/file scan balance heuristic now uses vmstat-derived scan-balance costs, which are read lock-free from per-LRU vmstats and folded into a per-lruvec accumulator. This change improves performance on memory-pressured workloads by reducing contention on lru_lock, but introduces some trade-offs such as slightly worse signal freshness and a small increase in per-lruvec footprint.

[PATCH v2] smp: Use release stores for csd_lock_record() state Neutral llm-chunked-no-replies

This patch updates the code in kernel/smp.c to use release stores instead of explicit memory barriers and plain stores when publishing per-CPU CSD (Centralized System Data) debug state. The change ensures that the preceding cur_csd_func and cur_csd_info fields are ordered before the pointer is published, matching the smp_load_acquire() used in csd_lock_wait_toolong(). This replaces open-coded smp_wmb() plus plain cur_csd store with a release operation. Additionally, the clear path uses smp_store_release(&cur_csd, NULL) to maintain ordering after callback/unlock work without requiring a full barrier before the store.

Last comment 2026-06-24 Re: [PATCH v4 4/5] mm/memcontrol: convert memcg to use page_counter_stock Neutral LLM (per-reviewer)

This patch converts memcg to use page_counter_stock instead of the original hierarchical page_counter walk for uncharging. The change is motivated by concerns about stock growing without bound when using a hierarchical walk, and aims to maintain consistency with the original memcontrol code.

3 reviewer comments — 0 participants
0 participants
Joshua Hahn ↳ replying to Usama Arif Reviewed-by Needs Work LLM
reviewer noted that page_counter_try_charge_stock() can succeed by consuming memsw stock and charging 0 new pages, but the memory-failure path unconditionally uncharges nr_pages from memsw, turning a failed allocation into a real memsw usage decrement, and requested a transactional rollback to fix this issue Reviewer Joshua Hahn expressed uncertainty about whether page_counter_try_charge_stock() can succeed by consuming memsw stock and charging 0 new pages, but the memory-failure path unconditionally uncharges nr_pages from memsw, potentially turning a failed allocation into a real memsw usage decrement. He suggested that this might be a distinction between cgroup v1 with memsw and not using cgroup v1 with memsw, or alternatively, that the issue is with taking a charge via stock but uncharging with a hierarchical page_counter walk.
Show original comment 
Hello Usama!!

Thank you for reviewing the patch : -)

[...snip...]

---

Hmmmmmmmmmm....... I'm not sure.

At this point in the code, we are either (1) using cgroup v1 with memsw
and charged successfully, or (2) not using cgroup v1 with memsw. So I'm
not sure if this really is unconditional, we're just distinguishing
between cases (1) and (2) by checking if we're using cgroupv1.

Or is your concern with taking a charge via stock, but uncharging with
a hierarchical page_counter walk? If so, I think there's a case to be
made here with just simply returning the stock. I just wanted to keep
it consistent with the original memcontrol code, which only used
stock to fulfill charges, not uncharges, since this could make the
stock grow without bound.

What do you think? Thanks again for reviewing Usama, I hope you have a
great day!!!
Joshua

This patch modifies the csd_lock_record() function in the Linux kernel's SMP implementation to use release stores instead of smp_wmb(). The change aims to improve performance by removing unnecessary memory barriers, as documented in the kernel source code.
